منهاج كونها معلومة، وقولكم الشر المحض ليس بموجود أصلا، فإن الوجود اشتمل على الخير) أو هو خير كله.
فنقول: أثبتم ترتيبا في الوجود حتى قضيتم بأن الوجود في بعض الموجودات أول وأولى، وفي بعضها لا أول ولا أولى، فهلا أثبتم فيه تضادًا أيضا حتى تحكموا بأن الوجود في بعض الموجودات خير كله وفي بعضها شر كله، وقد سمعتم من أصحاب الشرائع إثبات الملائكة الروحانيين وذلك خير كله، وإثبات الشياطين وهذا شر كله وأصحاب الأصلين النور والظلمة يقرون عليكم السلام للإلزام وأصحاب الكلام يلزمونكم إثبات موجودات العالم غير مستندة إلى الموجد كأنها وقعت اتفاقا لا قصدًا، أو حدثت فلتة لا إرادة واختيارًا، فإن كانت لا مستند لها فهي مخلوقة لا خالق لها، وإن كان مستندها خيرًا محضا، فكيف يصدر الشر من الخير؟ وإن كان مستندها شراً محضاً فالشر المحض لا وجود له عندكم، فما الجواب؟ وكيف الخروج عن عهدة الخطاب؟
ونقول: نرى العالم الجسماني مملوءاً بالبلايا والمحن والرزايا والفتن، ومشحونا بالآفات والعاهات والطوارق والحسرات ومتموجا بالجهالات وفاسد الاعتقادات، وأكثر الخلق على الأخلاق الذميمة والخصال اللئيمة، واستيلاء القوة الشهوية والغضبية على العقلية، حتى لا تكاد تجد في قرن من القرون إلا واحدًا يقول بالحكمة الإلهية التي هي التشبه بالإله عندكم أو فرقة يسيرة ترتسم بالمراسيم الشرعية التي هي امتثال الأوامر الإلهية عندنا، بل أكثرهم ( صم بكم عمى يعقلون) (البقرة:166)، ( وما وجدنا لأكثرهم من عهد وإن وجدنا أكثرهم لفسقين) (الأعراف:102)، ( وقليل من عبادي الشكور) (سبأ:13)، فكيف يستمر لكم معاشر الفلاسفة أن الشر في العالم بمطلقه لا يوجد وبأكثره لا يتحقق، وقد صادفتم الوجود على خلاف ما قررتم، فاعتبروا النفوس الإنسانية والغالب على أحوالها من العلم والجهل وعلى أخلاقها من الحسن والقبيح، وعلى أقوالها من الصدق والكذب، وعلى أفعالها من الشر والخير تعلمون أن الشر في العالم الجسماني أغلب وأكثر خصوصا في النفوس الإنسانية.
وبالجملة فحيث ما وجدنا الفطرة والتقدير الإلهي غالبا على الاختيار والاكتساب البشري، كان الغالب هو الخير والصلاح، وحيث ما وجدنا الاختيار والاكتساب غالبين كان الغالب هو الشر والفساد، فعاد الأمر إلى أن لا شر في الأفعال الإلهية، فإن وجد فيها شر فبالإضافة إلى شيء دون شيء، وإنما يدخل الشر في الأفعال الإنسانية الاختيارية وهي أيضا من حيث إنها تستند إلى إرادة الباري سبحانه خير، ومن حيث إنها تستند إلى اكتساب العبد تكتسب اسم الشر، غير أن الشرائع قد وردت بإثبات الشياطين وإثبات رأسهم إبليس اللعين، وليس يمكن إنكار ذلك بعد ثبوت الصدق في أقوالهم وأخبارهم بالبينات الواضحة والمعجزات الباهرة، وقد اعترف بوجودها قدماء الحكماء :قالوا : ما من شيء جزئي في العالم إلا ويتحقق له في عالم آخر أمر كلي، فبالحرارة الجزئية استدل على نار كلية.
The method of its being known. And your statement that pure evil does not exist at all, for existence includes good, or it is all good. We say: You have established an ordering in existence so that you judge that existence in some existents is first and more worthy, and in some it is neither first nor more worthy. Why do you not also establish opposition in it so that you judge that existence in some existents is all good and in some is all evil? And you have heard from the people of the laws the affirmation of spiritual angels, and that is all good, and the affirmation of devils, and that is all evil. The followers of the two principles of light and darkness acknowledge you with peace for the sake of refutation, and the theologians oblige you to affirm that the existents of the world are not dependent on the Existent, as if they occurred by chance not by intention, or happened randomly without will and choice. If they have no support, then they are created without a creator. If their support is pure good, how can evil issue from good? And if their support is pure evil, then pure evil has no existence according to you. So what is the answer? And how to escape the responsibility of the speech? And we say: We see the corporeal world filled with calamities, tribulations, misfortunes, and temptations, and packed with afflictions, defects, sudden events, and regrets, and surging with ignorances and corrupt beliefs. Most of creation are upon blameworthy morals and base traits, and the predominance of the appetitive and irascible powers over the rational, to the extent that you hardly find in any century except one who speaks of divine wisdom, which is assimilation to the divine according to you, or a small group that adheres to the religious laws, which are compliance with divine commands according to us. Rather, most of them are deaf, dumb, blind, they do not reason (Quran 2:166). And We did not find for most of them any covenant, and indeed We found most of them defiantly disobedient (Quran 7:102). And few of My servants are grateful (Quran 34:13). So how can you, O philosophers, continue that evil in the world absolutely does not exist and in its majority is not realized, when you have encountered existence contrary to what you determined? So consider the human souls and the predominant state of their condition regarding knowledge and ignorance, and their morals regarding good and bad, and their statements regarding truth and falsehood, and their actions regarding evil and good; you will know that evil in the corporeal world is more prevalent and more, especially in human souls. In summary, wherever we find innate disposition and divine decree predominant over human choice and acquisition, the predominant is good and righteousness; and wherever we find choice and acquisition predominant, the predominant is evil and corruption. So the matter returns to there being no evil in divine acts; if evil is found in them, it is relative to something not something else. Evil only enters into human voluntary actions, and they too are good inasmuch as they are based on the will of the Blessed and Exalted, and inasmuch as they are based on the acquisition of the servant they acquire the name of evil. However, the religious laws have come with the affirmation of devils and the affirmation of their leader Iblis the accursed, and it is not possible to deny that after the verification of truth in their statements and reports with clear proofs and dazzling miracles. The ancient sages acknowledged their existence, saying: There is no particular thing in the world except that there is realized for it in another world a universal command; thus from particular heat they inferred a universal fire.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation of the philosophical position that pure evil does not exist. The author begins by quoting the philosophers' claim that existence includes only good or is entirely good. He then counters by pointing out that if the philosophers admit an ordering (tarīb) in existence, they should also admit opposition (taḍād), so that some existents are all good and others all evil. He invokes the religious acknowledgment of angels (all good) and devils (all evil), and notes that the dualists (aṣḥāb al-aṣlayn) also affirm good and evil principles. The author then presents a dilemma: either the world's existents have no support (meaning they are created without a creator) or their support is pure good or pure evil. If the support is pure good, how does evil come from it? If pure evil, then pure evil does not exist according to them. Thus, the philosophers are forced to answer. Next, the author provides empirical evidence from the corporeal world, listing calamities, moral failings, and the rarity of genuine believers, citing Quranic verses to show that evil is prevalent. He concludes with a key distinction: when divine decree and innate disposition dominate, good prevails; when human choice and acquisition dominate, evil prevails. Therefore, there is no evil in God's acts; evil is only in human voluntary actions, which are good in relation to God's will and evil in relation to human acquisition. He also defends the reality of devils and Satan as affirmed by scripture and acknowledged by ancient sages, showing that evil beings exist in the spiritual world.

Key Terms
شر (sharr)
شياطين (shayāṭīn)
إبليس (Iblīs)
معجزات (muʿjizāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the existence of devils undeniable?
A) Because they are visible in the world.
B) Because religious laws affirmed it and their truth is proven by miracles.
C) Because ancient philosophers proved it logically.
D) Because everyone believes in them.

Key Takeaway
Devils and evil spirits are real, confirmed by revelation and supported by ancient philosophical principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
If ancient sages could infer universal realities from particular examples, what does your own experience of evil in the world lead you to conclude about the spiritual realm? How does your life reflect the seriousness of this unseen war?

Refuting Dualists and Philosophers on Good and Evil
Refutes dualists and philosophers on good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the dualistic and philosophical views on good and evil by identifying their key claims and showing their insufficiency.

Core Idea
Good and evil cannot arise from separate origins or chance; all traces back to Allah's wisdom.

In this passage, the author takes on two opposing groups: the dualists who posit two eternal origins (light and darkness) for good and evil, and the philosophers who deny a pure source of evil and claim good is predominant. He summarizes their positions and points out that unless their principles are established, their arguments collapse. This lesson unpacks the three key points of disagreement with the philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups
Read the passage and note that the author addresses both the Magians/Dualists and the Philosophers. Understand their basic claims.
The dualists are mentioned first, then the philosophers.
2
Understand the dualist position
The Dualists affirm two origins: light (source of good) and darkness (source of evil). This is a summary; the author references his other book for details.
3
Examine the three states of philosophical disagreement
The philosophers differ from the author in three specific ways. List them and consider what each entails.
These three are the core of the philosophical position.
4
Grasp the conditional argument
The author argues that unless these three principles are proven, the philosophers' overall position is incomplete. This is a logical refutation.
This shows the author's method of critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك أثبت المجوس وأصحاب الاثنين للعالم أصلين هما منبع الخير والشر والنفع والضرر وهما النور والظلمة كما سبق ذكر مذاهبهم، وقد استوفيناها في كتابنا الموسوم بالملل والنحل وبالجملة الفلاسفة منازعون في ثلاثة أحوال: أولها نفي محض موجود هو أصل الشر بالذات لا بالعرض والثاني كون الخير في النوع الإنساني أكثر وأغلب والثالث إثبات موجودات لا مستند لها على طريق الإيجاد بالذات بالقصد الأول، وما لم تثبت هذه الأصول لم يتم لهم ما ذكروه، والله أعلم وأحكم.
Likewise, the Magians and the Dualists affirmed for the world two origins that are the source of good and evil, benefit and harm, namely light and darkness, as their doctrines were previously mentioned. And we have covered them fully in our book titled 'Al-Milal wa l-Nihal'. In sum, the philosophers disagree in three states: first, the negation of a pure existent that is the origin of evil by essence not by accident; second, that good is more abundant and prevalent in the human species; third, the affirmation of existents that have no basis by way of origination by essence with primary intent. And unless these principles are established, what they mentioned is not completed for them. And God knows best and is most wise.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualists (Magians) and philosophers. The author first notes that the Magians and Dualists (those who posit two principles) affirm two origins for the world: light and darkness, which are the sources of good and evil, benefit and harm. He references his earlier book 'Al-Milal wa l-Nihal' for a fuller account. Then he states that the philosophers are disputants in three states (or conditions): first, they deny a pure existent that is the essence of evil; second, they claim that good is more abundant in humans; third, they affirm existents that have no basis of origination. The author argues that unless these three principles are proven, the philosophers' overall argument fails. He concludes with 'And God knows best'.

الخير
الشر

إثبات موجودات لا مستند لها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualists believe that good and evil come from two separate eternal origins (light and darkness).
Correction: Islam affirms only one eternal Creator; evil is a relative privation of good, not an independent principle.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If not, what hidden assumption about Allah's justice might you be holding that contradicts this lesson?
?
The author argues that unless certain principles are proven, the philosophers' arguments collapse. What unproven assumptions about good and evil do you carry in your own heart? Are you willing to examine them in the light of Allah's transcendence, or do you cling to them out of pride or comfort?

Refuting the Composition of Divine Speech from Letters
Refutation through two impossible scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine speech cannot be composed of letters and sounds like human speech, and how two scenarios lead to absurd conclusions.

Core Idea
God's speech is beyond physical composition; any attempt to liken it leads to incarnation.

The author argues that if God's speech were composed of letters and sounds, it would require a physical medium. This leads to two impossible scenarios: either the medium has human organs, implying incarnation, or it lacks organs, reducing speech to meaningless noise. This passage establishes the transcendence of divine speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure of two conditional scenarios.
The author uses a conditional 'either...or' structure.
2
Analyze the first scenario
Identify what happens if the medium includes articulation organs. Trace the chain of consequences leading to incarnation (ḥulūl).
This scenario leads to anthropomorphism.
3
Analyze the second scenario
Identify what happens if the medium lacks articulation organs. Note that the speech becomes mere sounds, indistinguishable from natural noises.
This contradicts the conventional meaning of speech.
4
Synthesize the refutation
Understand that both possibilities are impossible for God, so divine speech cannot be composed of letters and sounds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثل الكلام في اللسان فإن هذا مركب من حروف منظومة، والحروف تقطيع أصوات، والأصوات اصطكاك أجرام، والباري سبحانه تعالى عن أن يكون جرماً أو مركبا من جرم وجسم وإن قدر كلامه يخلقه في محل لم يكن ذلك الكلام قولا له، بل فعلا، ونحن نوافقكم في التعريفات الفعلية على أن هذا التقدير يستحيل أيضا، فإن ذلك المحل إما أن يشتمل على مخارج الحروف أو لا يشتمل، فإن اشتمل وجب أن يتحقق فيه حياة فيكون ذا بنية وهيئة إنسانية فيكون إنسانًا، فيكون الباري تعالى متكلما بلسان بشر، فيكون قد تصور بصورة البشر، فيلزم أن يقال: إنه يسمع بسمعه ويبصر ببصره كما نطق بلسانه ويأخذ بيده ويمشي برجله، وهذا هو الحلول الذي ذهب إليه أصحابه. وإن لم يشتمل على مخارج الحروف كانت أصواتاً مجردة لا حروفًا منظومة فلا يكون كلامه من جنس كلامنا، بل الأصوات الحادثة من حفيف الشجر ودوي الماء وصوت الرعد يكون كلامًا له، وذلك خلاف الكلام المتعارف.
Like speech in the tongue, for this is composed of ordered letters, and letters are the cutting of sounds, and sounds are the collision of bodies. The Exalted Creator is far above being a body or composed of body and mass. And if His speech is supposed to be created by Him in a locus, that speech would not be a saying of His, but rather an act. And we agree with you in the definition of acts that this supposition is also impossible. For that locus either includes the articulation organs or does not. If it includes them, then life must be realized in it, so it would have a structure and human form, so it would be a human. Then the Exalted Creator would be speaking with the tongue of a human, so He would have taken on the form of a human. Consequently, it must be said: He hears with His hearing, sees with His sight, just as He spoke with His tongue, and takes with His hand, and walks with His leg. This is the incarnation (al-ḥulūl) which its adherents held. And if it does not include the articulation organs, then they would be mere sounds, not ordered letters, so His speech would not be of the genus of our speech. Rather, the accidental sounds from the rustling of trees, the roaring of water, and the sound of thunder would be speech of His, and that is contrary to conventional speech.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that God's speech could be like human speech composed of letters and sounds. The author argues by presenting two exclusive scenarios. First, if God created speech in a physical medium that possesses the organs of articulation (makhārij al-ḥurūf), then that medium would have to be alive and have a human structure, leading to the conclusion that God would be speaking through a human tongue, hearing with human ears, and even walking. This is the anthropomorphic doctrine of incarnation (ḥulūl), which is impossible for God. Second, if the medium lacks articulation organs, then the sounds produced would not be composed letters but mere noises, and even rustling trees and thunder would be called God's speech, which contradicts the conventional understanding of speech. Thus, both possibilities are absurd, proving that God's speech is not of this physical, letter-based type.

Key Terms
حلول
مخارج الحروف
حروف منظومة
أصوات مجردة
كلام متعارف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's speech could be like human speech composed of letters and sounds if He creates it in a physical medium.
Correction: This leads to either incarnation (if the medium has human organs) or the absurdity that natural sounds are God's speech (if it lacks organs). Neither is acceptable for God's transcendence.

Checkpoint
What does the first scenario (the medium includes articulation organs) lead to?
A) The speech becomes mere sounds.
B) The medium must be a living human, leading to incarnation.
C) The speech becomes psychic speech.
D) The speech is not created.

Key Takeaway
Divine speech cannot be composed of letters and sounds because any physical medium leads to impossible consequences, preserving God's transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refutation of Psychic Speech as Divine Attribute
Objection and answer: two types of psychic speech refuted

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God's speech is psychic speech is impossible and how the author refutes it through two distinct analyses.

Core Idea
Psychic speech still implies temporal processes, which are impossible for Allah.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection: perhaps God's speech is inner psychic speech rather than literal letters and sounds. He carefully defines two forms of psychic speech and shows both entail temporal mental actions, thus impossible for the eternal Creator. The conclusion establishes a crucial principle: divine speech is not of the same genus as human speech, so analogical reasoning from human speech to divine speech fails.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the objection
Read the opening phrase 'فإن قيل' (if it is said) which introduces the opposing view that God's speech is psychic speech.
This is a common method in kalam: presenting and then refuting an objection.
2
Understand the two types of psychic speech
The author divides psychic speech into two types. The first involves learned mental representations of letters and words. The second involves innate discrimination and thought that is expressed verbally. Note the why each is impossible for God.
Both types imply sequential processes (beginning, middle, end) which are incompatible with God's timeless nature.
3
Grasp the final principle
The author concludes that God's speech is not of the genus of human speech at all. Therefore, one cannot use analogical reasoning (qiyās) from human speech to prove anything about divine speech, because 'what is not of the genus does not indicate the genus.'
This is a key logical principle: different genera do not serve as evidence for each other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: إن كلامه من جنس النطق النفساني فهو باطل أيضًا، فإن النطق النفسي على وجهين: أحدهما: ما هو مأخوذ في اللسان عربياً وعجميًّا، وغير ذلك إلى ما يعلم من المعلمين فيكون ذلك تقديرات حروف في الخيال وتصويرات كلمات في الوهم ويستحيل أن يكون كلامه تعالى من جنس المأخوذات. والثاني: ما هو مركوز في طبيعة النفس من التمييز والتفكير بحيث يعبر عنه باللسان فيكون هو مأخوذ اللسان، وذلك أيضا مستحيل؛ لأن ذلك يقتضي ترديد الخواطر وتقليب الأفكار والابتداء من مبدأ والانتهاء إلى منتهى، وذلك كله محال، فثبت أنه لا يجوز أن يكون من جنس كلام البشر، وما كان خارجا عن كلام البشر لا يجوز أن يثبت بالاستدلال على كلام البشر؛ لأن غير الجنس لا يدل على الجنس.
For if it is said: 'His speech is of the genus of psychic speech (al-nāṭiq al-nafsānī),' it is also false. For psychic speech is of two types: One of them is that which is taken in the tongue, Arabic and non-Arabic, and other such things learned from teachers. That consists of estimations of letters in the imagination and depictions of words in the fancy, and it is impossible for His speech, exalted, to be of the genus of things taken. The second type is that which is ingrained in the nature of the soul, consisting of discrimination and thinking, such that it is expressed by the tongue, so it is what the tongue takes. That is also impossible, because it requires the repetition of thoughts, the turning over of ideas, the beginning from a starting point and the ending at an endpoint, and all of that is impossible. So it is established that it is not permissible for His speech to be of the genus of human speech. And what is outside human speech cannot be proven by inference from human speech, because what is not of the genus does not indicate the genus.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer sequence. The objector suggests that God's speech might be psychic speech (inner speech). The author refutes it by analyzing two definitions of psychic speech. The first type is speech that is learned from language (e.g., Arabic, Persian) and consists of mental representations of letters and words. This is impossible for God because it implies acquisition and mental processes. The second type is the innate ability of discrimination and thought that is expressed verbally. This also implies sequential mental acts (beginning, middle, end), which are impossible for the timeless God. The author concludes that God's speech is not of the genus of human speech at all, and therefore one cannot use analogical reasoning (qiyās) from human speech to prove anything about divine speech, because different genera do not indicate each other.

Key Terms
نطق نفساني
تقديرات حروف
ترديد الخواطر
تقليب الأفكار
غير الجنس لا يدل على الجنس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that describing God's speech as 'psychic speech' avoids anthropomorphism and is acceptable.
Correction: The author shows that psychic speech still implies temporal mental processes and composition, which are impossible for the eternal and simple God.

Checkpoint
Why is the second type of psychic speech (innate discrimination and thought) impossible for God?
A) It requires learning from teachers.
B) It involves sequential mental processes.
C) It is only possible for humans.
D) It uses letters and sounds.

Key Takeaway
God's speech is entirely unlike human speech, so we cannot reason about it by analogy with human speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The refutation here shows that even seemingly 'spiritualized' concepts of speech (such as psychic speech) fall short of Allah's transcendence. Have you ever been tempted to reduce Allah to a concept that feels comfortable or understandable? How will you train your mind to embrace His absolute otherness, even when it feels distant or challenging?

Divine Speech Is Not of Human Genus
Refutation of divine speech being like human speech due to temporal processes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine speech cannot be of the same genus as human speech, based on the impossibility of temporal processes in God.

Core Idea
Divine speech is beyond human genus; temporal thought processes cannot apply to God.

The author presents a second argument against likening divine speech to human speech. Even if human speech is defined as an inner faculty of thought expressed by the tongue, it entails temporal sequences incompatible with God's eternal nature. Therefore, divine speech cannot be classified under the genus of human speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Focus on the description of human speech and why it is impossible for God.
2
Identify the impossibility
Note that human speech involves repetition of thoughts, turning over ideas, and beginning and ending. These are temporal processes.
The author calls all that 'impossible' for God.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that divine speech cannot be of the genus of human speech.
This is the key point of this lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: ما هو مركوز في طبيعة النفس من التمييز والتفكير بحيث يعبر عنه باللسان فيكون هو مأخوذ اللسان، وذلك أيضا مستحيل ؛ لأن ذلك يقتضي ترديد الخواطر وتقليب الأفكار والابتداء من مبدأ والانتهاء إلى منتهى، وذلك كله محال، فثبت أنه لا يجوز أن يكون من جنس كلام البشر، وما كان خارجا عن كلام البشر لا يجوز أن يثبت بالاستدلال على كلام البشر؛ لأن غير الجنس لا يدل على الجنس، فيخرج على هذا قولكم : إن كل عالم يجد من نفسه خبرًا عن معلومه، فإن هذا وإن سلم في الشاهد فكيف يستمر لكم طرده في الغائب مع تسليمكم أن الخبرين لا يتماثلان إلا في اسم الخبرية. وكذلك قولكم : إن كل ملك مطاع يجب أن يكون صاحب أمر بالتكليف، فإنه مسلم في الشاهد على ما ذكرنا غير مطرد في الغائب على المنهج الذي هو معتاد في الشاهد، وهذا الإلزام ليس يختص بمسألة الكلام، بل متوجه على من يجمع بين الشاهد والغائب في معنى لا يشتركان فيه جنساً ومماثلة، ويكون حكمه حكم من يجمع بين قرص الشمس ومنبع الماء، لا في معنى يشتركان فيه جنساً ومماثلة، بل بمجرد اسم العين المطلق عليهما بالاشتراك.
And the second: that which is ingrained in the nature of the soul of discernment and thought such that it is expressed by the tongue, so it is the taken of the tongue, and that also is impossible; because that entails repetition of thoughts and turning over of ideas and beginning from a starting point and ending at an end, and all that is impossible. So it is established that it is not permissible for it to be of the genus of human speech. And what is outside the genus of human speech is not permissible to be proven by inference on human speech; because what is not of the genus does not indicate the genus. Hence, your statement follows: that every knower finds in himself a report about his known thing, for even if this is conceded in the witnessed, how can you extend it to the unseen while you acknowledge that the two reports are not identical except in the name of reportness? And likewise your statement: that every obeyed king must be an owner of a command of obligation, for it is conceded in the witnessed as we mentioned, not extendable to the unseen on the method that is customary in the witnessed. And this necessary implication is not specific to the issue of speech, but rather applies to whoever combines the witnessed and the unseen in a meaning in which they do not share in genus and similarity, and his judgment is like the judgment of one who combines the disk of the sun and the source of water, not in a meaning in which they share in genus and similarity, but merely by the name 'ayn (entity) applied to them by homonymy.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the opponent's attempt to liken divine speech to human speech. The author presents the second impossibility: even if human speech is defined as an innate faculty of discrimination and thought expressed by the tongue, it would still be impossible for God because it entails temporal processes like repeating thoughts and beginning and ending. Thus, divine speech cannot be of the same genus as human speech. The author then argues that what is not of a genus cannot be proven by reasoning based on that genus. He then refutes the opponent's analogical arguments from the witnessed to the unseen. The opponent's claim that every knower has a report about his known object is conceded for humans but cannot be extended to God because the two reports (human and divine) are similar only in name. Similarly, the claim that every obeyed king must issue commands is conceded for human kings but does not apply to God in the same way. This error of combining the witnessed and unseen in a mere homonymy is like comparing the sun and a water spring just because both are called 'ayn (eye/source). The author stresses that this fallacy is not limited to the issue of speech but applies to any such invalid analogy.

كلام البشر
الجنس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if human speech involves inner thought, then divine speech could be similar.
Correction: The author shows that this involves temporal processes, impossible for God.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author gives for why divine speech cannot be of the genus of human speech?
A) Human speech is always verbal.
B) Human speech involves temporal processes like repetition and beginning-ending.
C) Human speech is imperfect.
D) Divine speech is not audible.

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is not of the genus of human speech because it would entail temporal processes incompatible with God's eternal nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Have you approached it as a set of human-like commands and stories, rather than an eternal reality that calls you to transcend your own limited thinking? Name one change you must make today in how you hear or recite it.

Invalidity of Analogy Between Witnessed and Unseen
Refutation of analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen without shared genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen fails when the two terms do not share a genus, as illustrated by the homonymy example.

Core Idea
Analogy requires shared genus; mere homonymy does not prove similarity.

Having established that divine speech is not of human genus, the author now addresses the opponent's analogical arguments from the witnessed to the unseen. He argues that such reasoning is invalid unless the two sides share a genus. He gives examples and compares the error to calling both the sun and a water source by the name 'ayn (eye/source) without implying similarity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation from 'And what is outside the genus' to the end.
2
Identify the fallacy
The author states that what is not of a genus cannot be proven by inference from that genus. Then he criticizes the opponent's analogies.
The opponent uses analogies from human experience to God.
3
Analyze the examples
The opponent says every knower has a report about his known thing, and every obeyed king issues commands. The author says these are conceded for humans but not extendable to God.
The two reports or commands are similar only in name, not in genus.
4
Understand the homonymy analogy
The author compares this error to calling the sun and a water source both 'ayn, which means they share only a name, not any real similarity.
This emphasizes that mere homonymy does not constitute a valid analogy.

The Analogy of the Sun and Colored Glasses
The philosopher answers an objection about emanation from the one intellect using an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosopher uses the analogy of sunlight and colored glasses to explain that multiplicity arises from receivers, not the source, and how this challenges the Ash'ari position on divine attributes.

Core Idea
Multiplicity arises from receivers, not the source.

The philosopher responds to a critical objection: if the First Intellect is absolutely one, how can multiple different things emanate from it? He offers a powerful analogy—sunlight shining on glasses of different colors. Each glass receives the light differently, producing different colors without any change in the sun. He then applies this logic to the Ash'ari view that God's speech is one entity yet contains commands, prohibitions, etc., arguing that the multiplicity comes from the relations, not from God Himself. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: answer to an objection, analogy, application to divine speech, conclusion.
2
Understand the analogy
Recognize that the sun is one, its light is one, but different colored glasses produce different colors. The difference comes from the glasses, not the sun.
This is the core explanatory mechanism.
3
Connect to divine speech
Observe how the philosopher uses the same logic: God's speech is one, but its different properties (command, prohibition, etc.) come from the different relations, not from God.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The philosopher concludes that the parallel between emanation and divine attributes fails; the two cases are not equivalent in the way the objector assumed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يجيب المتفلسف عن إلزام الفيض المختلف عن العقل الواحد فيقول : أنا أقول : كما أن العقل واحد ففيض العقل أيضا واحد لكن القوابل والحوامل تختلف فيختلف الفيض باختلاف المفيض ؛ كالشمس إذا أشرقت على زجاجات مختلفة الألوان أعطت كل زجاجة لونا خاصا لائقًا بلونها القابل فتعدد الصور، واختلافها إنما حصل من جانب المفيض لا من جانب الفائض، وأنتم أثبتم كلاما واحدًا وهو في نفسه أمر ونهي وخبر واستخبار، فتعدد الخواص واختلافها فيه إنما حصل من جانب المتعلق لا من جانب المتكلم، فلم تستقم التسوية بين البابين بالتعلق والفيض.
Perhaps the philosopher answers the objection of different emanation from the one intellect and says: I say: just as the intellect is one, the emanation of the intellect is also one, but the receivers and substrates differ, so the emanation differs according to the difference of the recipient; like the sun when it shines on glasses of different colors, it gives each glass a specific color suitable to its receptive color, so the forms become multiple, and their difference arises from the side of the recipient, not from the side of the emanator. And you have affirmed a single speech which is in itself command, prohibition, news, and interrogation; so the multiplicity of properties and their difference in it arises from the side of the relation, not from the side of the speaker. Thus the equation between the two cases by relation and emanation does not hold.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a response from the philosopher to an objection about how multiple effects emanate from a single simple intellect. The philosopher explains that just as the intellect is one, its emanation is also one, but the receivers (قوابل) and substrates (حوامل) differ, so the emanation differs according to the recipient's capacity. He gives the analogy of sunlight shining on different colored glasses: each glass gives a specific color in accordance with its own receptive color. Thus multiplicity arises from the side of the recipient, not from the source. The philosopher then turns the argument against the Ash'ari denial of real attributes within God. He says: You (Ash'aris) affirm that God's speech is one entity, yet in itself it is command, prohibition, news, and interrogation. So the multiplicity of properties arises from the side of the relation (المتعلق), not from the side of the speaker. The philosopher concludes that the parallel between the two cases (emanation and divine attributes) is not consistent: the comparison via relation (التعلق) and emanation (الفيض) fails.

Key Terms
فيض
قوابل
حوامل
تعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the philosopher is denying the unity of God or introducing multiplicity in the source itself.
Correction: The philosopher argues that multiplicity arises only from the receivers, not from the source itself. The source remains one and unchanged.

Checkpoint
According to the philosopher's analogy, what causes the multiplicity of forms when sunlight shines on different colored glasses?
A) The sun itself
B) The glasses as receivers
C) The air between sun and glass
D) The color of the sunlight

Key Takeaway
The philosopher uses the analogy of sunlight and colored glasses to argue that multiplicity in effects arises from the receivers, not the source, and turns this argument against the Ash'ari view of divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosopher uses an analogy to explain how multiplicity arises without affecting the source. In your own life, do you tend to attribute the problems you face to external circumstances (the 'receivers') or to the source? Reflect on a time when you blamed a situation on someone else, only to realize later that the issue was within your own receptivity. How can you train your heart to be a better 'receiver' of divine guidance without distorting it?
?
The Ash'aris held that God's speech is one yet contains commands and prohibitions due to relations. Do you treat God's commands as a single coherent message, or do you pick and choose based on convenience? What would change if you truly believed that every command and prohibition in the Qur'an is one unified speech from the same source?
?
The philosopher points out that the equation between emanation and divine speech fails. In your understanding of God, are you tempted to create analogies that fit your own logic, even when they break down? How can you guard against reducing God to concepts that are comfortable for your intellect, rather than submitting to His revelation as it is?

The Refutation of Physical Manifestation Claims
The author dismisses physical manifestation claims and demands clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author dismisses claims that spiritual beings can appear in physical forms as empty words and why he demands clear explanation.

Core Idea
Spiritual beings cannot be physically represented; such claims are empty without clear meaning.

The author begins by addressing those who seek comfort in the idea that spiritual beings like angels can take physical form. He dismisses this as empty words and demands a clear explanation. This passage sets the stage for his own rational account of how angels appear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the author's strong opening dismissal.
The passage uses rhetorical questions.
2
Identify the author's reaction
The author says the claim is like 'empty words'. This is a direct refutation.
3
Understand the demand
He asks for clear definitions of 'representation' and 'formation'. This shows he requires conceptual precision.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Consider why the author rejects such claims before offering his own view.
This refutation is based on the principle that intelligible things do not bear unfounded claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما استرواحكم إلى تمثل الروحاني بالجسماني وتشكل الملك بالبشر، وظهور المعنى بالعبارات فذلك في أسماعنا شبه كلمات وكلمات فارغة، فما التمثل والتشكل؟ وكيف الظهور والتبين؟ حققوا لنا ذلك إن كانت العبارة مشتملة على حقيقة، وإلا فالمعلومات لا تحتمل أمثال هذه المجازفات. والذي عندنا أن جبريل شخص لطيف يتكاثف فيتراءى للبصر كالهوى اللطيف الذي لا يُرى، فيتكاثف فيرى سحابا، أو نقول بإعدام وإيجاد لا يتمثل ويتشخص، وبالجملة جوهر واحد لا يصير جواهر إلا بانضمام جواهر إليه ونحن لا نعقل من الجواهر إلا المتحيز، والمتحيزان لا يتداخلان، فلا معنى لما تمسكتم به.
As for your seeking relief by representing the spiritual by the physical and the angel taking the form of a human, and the appearance of meaning through expressions, that is in our ears like words and empty words. What is representation and formation? How is appearance and clarification? Clarify that for us if the expression contains a reality; otherwise, intelligible things do not bear such exaggerations. Our view is that Gabriel is a subtle being that becomes dense and appears to the eye like the subtle air that is not seen; it becomes dense and appears as a cloud. Or we say by annihilation and creation; it is not represented or personified. In short, a single substance does not become multiple substances except by the addition of other substances to it, and we do not conceive of substances except as occupying space, and two occupants of space cannot interpenetrate. So there is no meaning to what you have clung to.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who claim that spiritual beings (like angels) can physically manifest in human or other physical forms. The author dismisses such claims as empty words. He then presents his own position: Gabriel is a subtle being (lāṭif) that can become dense (yatakāthaf) and become visible, like a cloud. Alternatively, God may create and annihilate forms, but the angel itself does not undergo physical transformation. The key philosophical point is that substances (jawāhir) are defined by occupying space (taḥayyuz), and two such substances cannot interpenetrate. Therefore, the idea of an angel inhabiting a human body is incoherent.

Key Terms
تمثل
تشكل
روحاني
جسماني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Spiritual beings like angels can physically transform into bodies.
Correction: The author argues such claims are empty and demand clear definition; he will later provide a rational alternative.

Checkpoint
What does the author initially say about the claim that spiritual beings can take physical form?
A) It is false but worth considering.
B) It is like empty words.
C) It is possible if properly explained.
D) It is a known fact from revelation.

Key Takeaway
The author rejects physical manifestation claims as meaningless without clear definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a religious claim without demanding clear definitions, simply because it felt comfortable or familiar? What stopped you from asking 'What does that really mean?'
?
When you encounter teachings that seem to anthropomorphize God or spiritual beings, do you resist them with the same urgency as the author, or do you let them slide for the sake of ease? What would it cost you to insist on clarity?
?
If you were to apply the author's standard to your own understanding of faith, which of your beliefs would fail his test and be dismissed as 'empty words'? Are you ready to let them go?

The Author's Account: Gabriel as a Subtle Being and the Substance Argument
The author presents his positive view on angelic appearance and a philosophical argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's alternative explanation for how angels appear and the philosophical argument that substances cannot interpenetrate.

Core Idea
Gabriel appears by becoming dense like a cloud, not by physical transformation; two spatial substances cannot interpenetrate.

After dismissing the physical manifestation claim, the author presents his own view. He describes Gabriel as a subtle being that becomes dense to become visible. He then argues that a single substance cannot become multiple substances without addition, and because all substances occupy space, two such substances cannot interpenetrate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the two main parts: the Gabriel example and the substance argument.
2
Understand the Gabriel example
The author says Gabriel is a subtle being that becomes dense like air forming a cloud. Alternatively, God creates and annihilates forms.
This is an alternative to physical transformation.
3
Grasp the substance argument
A single substance cannot become multiple; substances are defined as occupying space; two spatial beings cannot interpenetrate.
This is a rational proof.
4
See how this refutes the claim
Since interpenetration is impossible, an angel cannot inhabit a body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما استرواحكم إلى تمثل الروحاني بالجسماني وتشكل الملك بالبشر، وظهور المعنى بالعبارات فذلك في أسماعنا شبه كلمات وكلمات فارغة، فما التمثل والتشكل؟ وكيف الظهور والتبين؟ حققوا لنا ذلك إن كانت العبارة مشتملة على حقيقة، وإلا فالمعلومات لا تحتمل أمثال هذه المجازفات. والذي عندنا أن جبريل شخص لطيف يتكاثف فيتراءى للبصر كالهوى اللطيف الذي لا يُرى، فيتكاثف فيرى سحابا، أو نقول بإعدام وإيجاد لا يتمثل ويتشخص، وبالجملة جوهر واحد لا يصير جواهر إلا بانضمام جواهر إليه ونحن لا نعقل من الجواهر إلا المتحيز، والمتحيزان لا يتداخلان، فلا معنى لما تمسكتم به.
As for your seeking relief by representing the spiritual by the physical and the angel taking the form of a human, and the appearance of meaning through expressions, that is in our ears like words and empty words. What is representation and formation? How is appearance and clarification? Clarify that for us if the expression contains a reality; otherwise, intelligible things do not bear such exaggerations. Our view is that Gabriel is a subtle being that becomes dense and appears to the eye like the subtle air that is not seen; it becomes dense and appears as a cloud. Or we say by annihilation and creation; it is not represented or personified. In short, a single substance does not become multiple substances except by the addition of other substances to it, and we do not conceive of substances except as occupying space, and two occupants of space cannot interpenetrate. So there is no meaning to what you have clung to.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who claim that spiritual beings (like angels) can physically manifest in human or other physical forms. The author dismisses such claims as empty words. He then presents his own position: Gabriel is a subtle being (lāṭif) that can become dense (yatakāthaf) and become visible, like a cloud. Alternatively, God may create and annihilate forms, but the angel itself does not undergo physical transformation. The key philosophical point is that substances (jawāhir) are defined by occupying space (taḥayyuz), and two such substances cannot interpenetrate. Therefore, the idea of an angel inhabiting a human body is incoherent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Angels can physically transform into human bodies.
Correction: The author shows that such transformation is impossible because substances cannot interpenetrate; angels appear by becoming dense, not by changing substance.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how does Gabriel appear to humans?
A) By physically transforming into a human body.
B) By becoming dense like a cloud.
C) By appearing in a dream.
D) By taking on a human form through incarnation.

Key Takeaway
The author argues that spiritual beings like Gabriel appear by becoming dense, not by physical transformation, and this is supported by the principle that substances cannot interpenetrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter spiritual truths that seem to defy physical laws, do you dismiss them as irrational, or do you humbly admit that your understanding of the physical world may be incomplete? How does the author's attempt to harmonize revelation and reason challenge your own approach to matters of faith?
?
The author insists that two substances cannot interpenetrate. Yet you daily witness the unseen realm—your own thoughts, intentions, and will—interacting with your physical body without occupying space. How does this lesson make you reconsider the relationship between the spiritual and material in your own life? Are you living as if the unseen is more real than the seen?
?
If Gabriel, a created being, can become dense and appear, what does that tell you about the Creator who is beyond density and subtlety altogether? How often do you reduce Allah to your own categories or limitations, and what concrete step will you take today to worship Him as the One who transcends all creaturely properties?

Refutation of Structure as a Condition for Life
Refutation of structure as a condition for life using consistency argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why structure cannot be a necessary condition for life in God, preserving divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Conditions for creatures cannot be imposed on the Creator without contradiction.

This passage deals with a key principle in Islamic theology: the distinction between conditions that apply to created beings (al-shāhid) and those that apply to God (al-ghā'ib). The author argues that if structure were a necessary condition for life, it would force us to attribute structure to God, which is impossible. This refutation protects God’s transcendence (tanzīh).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage slowly, then its English translation. Notice the logical flow: it starts with a distinction about accidents, then presents a conditional argument.
The passage uses technical terms like 'shāhid' and 'ghā'ib'.
2
Understand the Distinction About Accidents
The author first contrasts relational accidents that involve combination (like union and separation) with those that do not, such as perception of one's own attribute. This sets up the context for the main argument.
This part may be dense; focus on the key idea: some attributes don't require physical combination.
3
Grasp the Main Argument by Contradiction
The author says: if structure were a condition for life, then by consistent reasoning it would have to apply to God, making Him have structure. But that is impossible, so the condition is false.
This argument uses the principle of 'ṭard' (consistent application) in qiyās al-ghā'ib 'alā al-shāhid.
4
Reflect on Theological Implication
Understand that this argument protects God's transcendence. Conditions that apply to contingent beings cannot be transferred to God without leading to absurdity.
This is a fundamental principle in kalām for discussing divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو انضم إلى غيره، خصوصاً إذا كان الإدراك في صفة نفسه لا يقتضي جمعاً وإضافة ونسبة ولم يكن من الأعراض الإضافية بخلاف الاجتماع والافتراق والمماسة، ثم لو كانت البنية شرطًا كاشتراط كونه حيًا لوجب طرده في الغائب، حتى يكون الباري تعالى في علمه وقدرته ذا بنية كما كان حيًّا؛ لأن الشروط يجب طردها شاهدًا وغائبًا.
Or if it joins to another, especially if the perception in its own attribute does not require combination, addition, and relation, and it is not among the relational accidents, unlike union, separation, and contiguity. Then if structure were a condition, like the condition of being alive, then it would have to be applied in the unseen, so that the Most High Creator would have structure in His knowledge and power just as He is alive; because conditions must be applied consistently in the witnessed and the unseen.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that structure (al-bunya) is a necessary condition for life. The author first distinguishes between relational accidents that involve combination and addition versus those that do not. He then argues that if structure were a condition for life, like the condition of being alive, it would have to be applied consistently to both the witnessed (al-shāhid) and the unseen (al-ghā'ib). This would require that the Creator (al-Bārī) has structure in His knowledge and power, which is impossible and anthropomorphic. Therefore, the condition is invalid.

Key Terms

شرط
شاهد
غائب
عارض إضافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conditions that apply to created beings, such as needing structure for life, must also apply to God.
Correction: The text shows that applying such conditions to God leads to absurdity (attributing structure to Him), so those conditions are not transferable to the divine realm.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would follow if structure (al-bunya) were a condition for life?
A) God would not be alive
B) God would have structure like a body
C) The condition would not apply to the unseen
D) Perception would be impossible

Key Takeaway
Conditions that apply to created beings cannot be transferred to God without leading to absurdity, affirming divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's life, do you find yourself unconsciously imagining something like a living creature you know, with parts and movement? How does this argument confront that tendency, and what will you do to purge it from your heart?
?

?
This passage demands that you accept God's life without structure, without composition. Does that disturb you? Does it make God feel more distant or more real? Commit tonight to sitting with that discomfort and asking yourself why.

The Two Theories of Ru'ya: Perception Beyond Knowledge or Specific Knowledge?
Al-Shahrastani introduces the two theories of ru'ya and the Mu'tazila conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main theories about the nature of seeing Allah (ru'ya) and the Mu'tazila conditions for vision.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah is either a distinct perception or a specific type of knowledge.

Al-Shahrastani begins by clearing the ground: no Muslim has ever claimed that seeing Allah involves physical rays or impressions. The real debate is about the nature of the perception itself. Before delving into arguments, he summarizes the two competing theories and the conditions that some groups attach to vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the introduction and the clarification
Read the first part where al-Shahrastani states that no one from the qibla has claimed a physical connection between the eye and Allah's essence. This removes a false assumption.
Notice that the disagreement is not about physical contact but about the nature of perception.
2
Identify the two theories
The passage presents two views: vision is a perception beyond knowledge (idrak wara' al-'ilm) or a specific kind of knowledge ('ilm makhsus). Find these terms and understand the difference.
3
Understand the conditions of the Mu'tazila
Those who take the first theory differ on conditions: organ, ray connection, distance, and medium. The Mu'tazila conditioned these and thus denied ru'ya. Note that al-Ash'ari affirmed it.
4
Reflect on the transition
The author then says he will present the arguments of both groups. This sets up the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة السادسة عشرة في جواز رؤية الباري تعالى عقلا ووجوبها سمعا لم يصر صائر من أهل القبلة إلى تجويز اتصال أشعة من البصر بذاته تعالى، أو انطباع شبح يتمثل في الحاسة منه، وانفصال شيء من الرائي والمرئي واتصاله بهما، لكن أهل الأصول اختلفوا في أن الرؤية إدراك وراء العلم أم علم مخصوص، ومن زعم أنه إدراك وراء العلم اختلف في اشتراط البنية واتصال الشعاع، ونفي القرب المفرط والبعد المفرط وتوسط الهوى المشف، فشرطها المعتزلة ونفوا رؤية الباري تعالى بالأبصار نفي الاستحالة، والأشعري أثبتها إثبات الجواز على الإطلاق والوجوب بحكم الوعد، ثم ردد قوله: إنه علم مخصوص؛ أي لا يتعلق إلا بالموجود، أم هو إدراك حكمه حكم العلم في التعلق؛ أي لا يتأثر من المرئي ولا يؤثر فيه، ونحن نورد كلام الفريقين على الرسم المعهود. قالت الأشعرية: الموجودات اشتركت في قضايا واختلفت في قضايا، والرؤية قد تعلقت بالمختلفات منها والمتفقات ولا يجوز أن يكون المصحح للرؤية ما يختلف فيه فإنه يوجب أن يكون لحكم واحد علتان مختلفتان، وهذا غير جائز في
The sixteenth principle on the permissibility of seeing the Creator, exalted, intellectually and its obligation by revelation. No one among the people of the qibla held the permissibility of a connection of rays from the eye to His essence, exalted, or the imprinting of a phantasm that is represented in the sense from Him, or the separation of something from the seer and the seen and its connection to both. Rather, the people of principles differed on whether vision is a perception beyond knowledge or a specific knowledge. And whoever claimed that it is a perception beyond knowledge differed on the condition of the organ and the connection of the ray, and the negation of extreme proximity and extreme distance and the intermediacy of a transparent medium. The Mu'tazila conditioned them and denied seeing the Creator, exalted, with eyes as a negation of impossibility. Al-Ash'ari affirmed it as an affirmation of absolute permissibility and obligation by virtue of the promise. Then he vacillated on his statement: whether it is a specific knowledge, i.e., it is only related to the existent, or is it a perception whose ruling is the ruling of knowledge in relation, i.e., it is not affected by the seen nor does it affect it. And we will present the speech of the two groups according to the customary method. The Ash'aris said: Existents share in some matters and differ in some matters. Vision has been related to both their differing matters and their shared matters. It is not permissible that the corrector of vision be that in which they differ, for that would necessitate that a single judgment has two different causes, and that is not permissible in [the rest of the text is missing].
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage is the introduction to the sixteenth principle concerning the intellectual permissibility and revealed obligation of seeing Allah (ru'ya). The author first clarifies that no Muslim (from the qibla) has ever claimed that seeing Allah involves physical rays from the eye or an impression in the sense organ. This removes a common misunderstanding. Then he summarizes the disagreement among the people of principles (ahl al-usul) about the nature of ru'ya: is it a perception beyond knowledge (idrak wara' al-'ilm) or a specific kind of knowledge ('ilm makhsus)? Those who say it is a perception beyond knowledge differ on the conditions: the organ (al-binyah), connection of the ray (ittisal al-shu'a'), negation of excessive distance or proximity, and the presence of a transparent medium. The Mu'tazila conditioned these and thus denied the possibility of seeing Allah, negating it as impossible. Al-Ash'ari affirmed ru'ya as absolutely permissible and obligatory by virtue of the divine promise (of seeing Him in the afterlife). Then Ash'ari hesitated (taraddada) on whether ru'ya is a specific knowledge that only relates to the existent, or a perception whose ruling is like that of knowledge in that it neither affects nor is affected by the seen. Finally, the author states he will present the arguments of both groups. The Ash'ari argument given here is that existents share some attributes and differ in others. Vision has been observed to relate to both shared and differing attributes. Therefore, the corrector (musahhih) of the possibility of vision cannot be something in which existents differ, because that would give a single judgment (ru'ya) two different causes—an impossibility. This sets up the Ash'ari proof that the corrector must be something shared by all existents, namely existence itself, making it possible to see any existent, including Allah.

Key Terms
رؤية
إدراك
علم
جواز
وجوب
أهل الأصول
المعتزلة
الأشعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Mu'tazila denied ru'ya based solely on the impossibility of physical contact.
Correction: The passage shows that the Mu'tazila accepted the possibility of physical vision in principle but conditioned it on prerequisites (organ, ray, etc.) that cannot apply to Allah, leading to their denial.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main point of agreement among all Muslims regarding ru'ya?
A) That ru'ya is possible
B) That ru'ya does not involve physical rays or impressions
C) That ru'ya is a perception beyond knowledge
D) That ru'ya is obligatory by revelation

Key Takeaway
The debate on ru'ya is not about physical contact but about the nature of perception: whether it is a distinct perception or a type of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that you will see Allah, not out of blind imitation, but because you have internalized the promise and the arguments? What would change in your worship today if you were absolutely certain of that vision?
?
Al-Ash'ari hesitated on the nature of ru'ya. Have you ever hesitated in your own beliefs about Allah? What area of your faith feels most uncertain, and what is one step you can take this week to resolve it?
?
The Mu'tazila demanded conditions for vision that cannot apply to Allah. Are you imposing conditions on how Allah must deal with you based on your limited understanding? How can you surrender those conditions and trust His promise as He has revealed?

The Ash'ari Argument for Ru'ya: The Corrector Must Be Shared
The Ash'ari argument that the corrector of vision must be a shared attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the beginning of the Ash'ari proof that the corrector (musahhih) of vision must be something common to all existents, not something in which they differ.

Core Idea
Ru'ya's possibility depends on existence, not on differing attributes.

After introducing the two theories, al-Shahrastani presents the Ash'ari argument. The Ash'aris observe that vision applies to both shared and differing attributes of objects. Therefore, the factor that makes vision possible (the musahhih) cannot be something that varies among existents, because that would require multiple causes for a single judgment. This sets the stage for their conclusion that existence itself is the corrector.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari premise
Read the first sentence: 'The existents share in some matters and differ in some matters. Vision has been related to both their differing matters and their shared matters.' Understand that vision is observed to see things that are both alike and different.
2
Identify the logical implication
The argument states: if the corrector (musahhih) of vision were something in which existents differ, then a single judgment (vision) would have two different causes, which is invalid. This is a key logical move.
The passage does not complete the argument, but the implication is that the corrector must be something shared, i.e., existence.
3
Connect to the overall debate
This argument supports Al-Ash'ari's position that ru'ya of Allah is rationally permissible because Allah is existent. The Mu'tazila would need to deny that existence is the corrector.

Source Text

The Unique Nature of Hearing Among the Senses
The author answers the fifth objection by distinguishing the senses, especially hearing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the senses are not uniform and that hearing, unlike other senses, does not require physical contact, thus addressing an objection about the possibility of seeing God.

Core Idea
Hearing is pure perception without physical contact, distinguishing it from other senses.

In this passage, the author answers a key objection that might deny the possibility of seeing God based on the conditions of sensory perception. He argues that the senses are not all alike: each has its own unique property. In particular, he highlights hearing as a sense that does not require bodily contact, making it a pure perception. This distinction helps clarify why objections relying on sensory conditions do not apply universally.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the contrast between hearing and other senses.
2
Identify the key distinction
The author states that each sense has a unique property. Find where he explains that hearing does not require physical contact, unlike other senses.
The exception for hearing is crucial for the argument.
3
Understand the implication
Ask yourself: why does this distinction answer the fifth objection? The objection likely assumed all senses require contact; the author shows hearing is different, so the objection fails.
This connects to the broader theology of seeing God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن الاعتراض الخامس نقول: الرؤية من جملة الحواس الخمس، لكن الحواس مختلفة الحقائق والإدراكات ولكل حاسة خاصية لا يشركها فيها غيرها، وليس منها حاسة تتعلق بجنسي الجوهر والعرض على وتيرة واحدة، بل كلها تتعلق بأعراض ومن شرط تعلقها اتصال جسم بجسم حتى يحصل ذلك الإدراك عن السمع، فإنه لا يستدعي اتصال جسم بجسم ضرورة، بل هو إدراك محض كالرؤية.
As for the answer to the fifth objection, we say: Vision is among the five senses, but the senses have different realities and perceptions, and each sense has a property in which no other shares with it. None of them is a sense that relates to both substance and accident in one uniform manner. Rather, they all relate to accidents, and a condition of their relation is the contact of a body with a body, except for hearing, because it does not necessitate the contact of a body with a body necessarily, rather it is pure perception like vision.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is the author's answer to the fifth objection, which likely concerned the possibility of seeing God. The author argues that the senses are not uniform: each sense has a unique property. Specifically, hearing does not require physical contact between bodies, whereas other senses do. Thus, the objection that would deny vision based on sensory conditions is invalid. The author clarifies that hearing is pure perception, analogous to vision in that respect.

Correction: Hearing does not require physical contact; it is pure perception, as the author explicitly states.

Refutation of the Objection on Musa's Request for Ru'ya
The author refutes the claim that Musa asked for ru'ya for his people only.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that Prophet Musa asked to see God only as an argument for his people is inconsistent with the Quranic context and the principles of prophetic behavior.

Core Idea
Musa's request for ru'ya was genuine and personal, not a rhetorical device.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection raised by those who deny the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife. The objection suggests that Prophet Musa's request to see God was not for himself but was merely a rhetorical argument against his people who demanded to see God. The author refutes this by examining the Quranic verses and showing that such an interpretation contradicts the apparent meaning and prophetic standards.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the passage and find the initial objection: that Musa asked for ru'ya only for his people as an argument. Understand the claim.
2
Notice the first counterpoint
The author states the objection contradicts the apparent meaning of the Quran in every respect. Reflect on what 'apparent meaning' means in tafsir.
3
Examine the contextual evidence
Focus on the author's use of qarinat al-maqal (contextual evidence) from the verse 'But look at the mountain...' See how it proves the request was personal.
The verse shows a conditional: if the mountain remains, then you will see Me.
4
Compare with other Quranic requests
The author compares the response to Musa's request with the response to the people's request for an idol. Notice the immediate answer in the idol case, implying the same should happen if ru'ya were impossible.
5
Understand the Mu'tazila criticism
The author ends by criticizing the Mu'tazila for delaying clarification. Consider how this strengthens the refutation.
If ru'ya were impossible, God would have immediately refuted the doubt, as He did with the idol request.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: سأل الرؤية لقومه لا لنفسه، وإنما سألها إلزامًا عليهم بقول الله سبحانه: (لن تراني) حيث قالوا: (أرنا الله جهرة). قيل: هذا ما يخالف الظاهر من كل وجه، ومع مخالفته لا يجوز أن يسأل النبي سؤالًا محالًا لقومه، وقرينة المقال تدل على أن السؤال كان مقصورًا عليه من كل وجه؛ إذ قال: (ولكن انظر إلى الجبل فإن استقر مكانه فسوف تراني) إلى آخر الآية، ومنع موسى لا ينتهض إلزامًا على القوم، بل تقرير الحجة القاطعة على أن الله تعالى لا يجوز أن يكون مرئيًا يكون إلزامًا، فكيف نجا موسى من السؤال بلن تراني (ولكن انظر إلى الجبل)، ولم ينج قومه من السؤال في قولهم: (أرنا الله جهرة) إلا بالصاعقة المهلكة والعذاب الأليم، ولما قال قومه: اجعل لنا إلهًا كما لهم آلهة لم يلزمهم بالسؤال عن الله تعالى، بل أجابهم في الحال: (قوم يجهلون) إذ كان السؤال محالًا، فكذلك الرؤية لو كانت مستحيلة لأجابهم في الحال ورفع شبهتهم بالمقال، ومثل هذه الحالة لو قدرة المعتزلة ما استجازوا تأخير البيان عن وقت الحاجة ولعدوا سؤال المسئول من مسئول آخر اتهامًا آخر على شبهة قد تحققت لهم.
If it is said: He asked for the vision (al-ru'ya) for his people, not for himself, and he only asked it as an argument against them by the saying of God, may He be exalted: 'You will not see Me' when they said: 'Show us God publicly.' It is said: This contradicts the apparent meaning in every respect, and despite its contradiction, it is not permissible for the prophet to ask an impossible question for his people. The contextual evidence (qarinat al-maqal) indicates that the question was restricted to him in every respect, since he said: 'But look at the mountain; if it remains in its place, then you will see Me' to the end of the verse. And Musa's forbidding does not constitute an argument against the people. Rather, establishing the conclusive proof that God, exalted, cannot be visible would be an argument. So how did Musa escape from the question by 'You will not see Me' (and 'look at the mountain'), while his people did not escape from the question in their saying: 'Show us God publicly' except by the thunderbolt and painful punishment? And when his people said: 'Make for us a god as they have gods,' he did not burden them with the question about God, but answered them immediately: 'You are an ignorant people.' If the question was impossible, then likewise, if the vision (al-ru'ya) were impossible, He would have answered them immediately and removed their doubts with speech. And a similar situation, if the Mu'tazila had considered it, they would not have allowed delaying the clarification beyond the time of need, and they would have counted asking a questioned person from another questioner as a further accusation based on a doubt that had been confirmed for them.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation concerning the vision of God (ru'ya). The objection claims that Prophet Musa (peace be upon him) asked to see God not for himself but only as a rhetorical argument against his people who demanded to see God. The author refutes this by pointing out that the Quranic context clearly contradicts this interpretation: Musa's request is presented as his own, and the evidence (qarinat al-maqal) shows it was personal—he says 'look at the mountain' and 'you will see Me,' which would not make sense if he were only arguing. Moreover, if the vision were impossible, God would have immediately answered and refuted the people's doubts, just as He did when they asked for a physical idol. The author also criticizes the Mu'tazila for delaying clarification if they considered this argument, implying their position is inconsistent.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic context and prophetic conduct confirm that Musa's request to see God was a personal plea, not a rhetorical device.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The people demanded to see God publicly and were struck down. Have you ever demanded that Allah conform to your expectations rather than submitting to His majesty? Search your heart for any trace of arrogance in your worship. Repent and ask for humility.
?
God answered Musa's request with a condition. How do you respond when Allah delays or conditions your prayers? Do you trust His wisdom, or do you complain like the people? Resolve to accept Allah's timing and respond with patience.

Divine Actions Beyond Human Morality
Shahrastani argues that God's actions are not subject to human standards of good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Shahrastani argues that divine actions cannot be judged by human ethical standards.

Core Idea
God's actions are beyond human moral categories.

In this passage, Shahrastani tackles a central challenge to divine transcendence: if God rewards and punishes, must He conform to human notions of justice? He argues that the same act can be good from God and evil from us, showing that moral value depends on the agent's knowledge and purpose. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main question
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the opening question about whether reward and punishment are due from God.
The passage assumes God is beyond harm or benefit from actions.
2
Note the example of drowning and saving
The author gives the example of drowning (إيلام البريء) which is ugly from humans but not from God. Similarly, saving the drowning is good from us, but drowning is good from God. Reflect on why the same act can have opposite valuations.
This is the core analogy illustrating the argument.
3
Understand why analogy fails
The author concludes that acts have the same essential attributes, so the difference in moral judgment must come from the agent, not the act itself. Thus God's actions cannot be compared to human actions.
The passage ends by noting that disputes about rational matters arise from knowledge vs ignorance.

The act, in terms of its essential attributes, is one, so why is it ugly from one agent and good from another? And as for what they suppose of the disputants' disagreement on a rational matter, that, by my life, is among the approvals of the intellects insofar as one knows and the other is ignorant, not insofar as

This passage continues the response, now focusing on whether God owes reward or punishment. The same act (e.g., drowning) can be good from God and bad from us, showing that moral judgments are not intrinsic but depend on the agent. The passage concludes with an observation that disputes about rational matters often stem from knowledge vs ignorance.
