Divine Actions Beyond Human Measure
The author questions whether God must reward/punish and argues divine actions are incomparable to human ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine actions cannot be judged by human moral standards, and how this relates to the question of God's obligation to reward and punish.

Core Idea
Moral value is agent-relative: the same act can be good from God but evil from us.

This passage tackles a central question in Islamic theology: does God have to reward or punish human actions? The author argues that since God is beyond benefit and harm, and since His actions are incomparable to ours, we cannot apply human moral categories to Him. Before reading, consider how we normally judge actions as good or evil—the author challenges this by showing that the same act can be good from one agent and evil from another.

Guided Path
1
Read and orient
Read the passage and translation to grasp the overall argument. Note the key question about reward and punishment.
2
Identify the problem
See how the author questions our ability to know God's pleasure without revelation. What is the difficulty he raises?
The author argues we cannot know God's will without a truthful informant or indicative action.
3
Examine the examples
Focus on the examples: inflicting pain on innocents, destroying crops. Why are these evil for us but not for God?
The same type of act is judged differently depending on the agent.
4
Follow the hidden purpose argument
Understand the rebuttal about hidden purposes: if God has a secret purpose, so might we. Then conclude on essential attributes.
The final point: the act in itself is one, so why is it good from one and evil from another?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكنا فرضنا القول في حكم التكليف، هل يستحق على الله ثواب وعقاب بعد أن علم أنه لا يلحقه ضرر ولا نفع من فعله؟ وأما المتنازعان بالنفي والإثبات في أمر معقول قبل ورود الشرع وإنكار كل واحد منهما على صاحبه فمسلم، لكن الكلام وقع في حق الله تعالى هل يجب عليه أن يمدح ويذم ويثيب ويعاقب على ذلك الفعل وذلك غيب عنا. فبم يعرف أنه يرضى عن أحدهما ويثيبه على فعله ويسخط على الثاني ويعاقبه على فعله ولم يخبر عنه مخبر صادق ولا دل على رضاه وسخطه فعل ولا أخبر عن محكومه ومعلومه مخبر، ولا أمكن أن تقاس أفعاله على أفعال العباد؟ فإنا نرى كثيرًا من الأفعال تقبح منا ولا تقبح منه كإيلام البريء وإهلاك الحرث والنسل إلى غير ذلك، وعليه يخرج إنقاذ الغرقى والملكي، فإن نفس الإغراق والإهلاك يحسن منه تعالى ولا يقبح، وذلك منا قبيح والإنقاذ إن كان حسن فالإغراق يجب أن يكون قبيحا، فإن قدر في ضمن إهلاكه سر لم نطلع عليه أو غرض لم نوصله إليه إلا به فليقدر في إهلاكنا كذلك، والفعل من حيث الصفات النفسية واحد فلم قبح من فاعل وحسن من فاعل.
But we assumed the discussion concerning the rule of moral obligation (taklīf): Does reward and punishment become due from God, after it is known that no harm or benefit befalls Him from His action? As for the two disputants by negation and affirmation concerning a rational matter before the coming of revelation, and the denial of each one of them against his companion, it is conceded. However, the discussion concerns God Most High: Is it obligatory upon Him to praise and blame, and reward and punish for that action? That is hidden from us. So by what is it known that He is pleased with one of them and rewards him for his action, and is displeased with the other and punishes him for his action, when no truthful informant has informed about Him, and no action has indicated His pleasure or displeasure, and no informant has informed about His judged and known matters? And it is not possible that His actions be measured by the actions of servants? For we see many actions that are deemed evil (qabīḥ) from us but are not deemed evil from Him, such as inflicting pain on an innocent person, and destroying crops and progeny, and so forth. And accordingly, saving the drowning and the drowning are considered? For the very act of drowning and destroying is good (ḥasan) from Him Most High and not evil, while that from us is evil. And if saving is good, then drowning must be evil. But if it is posited that in His destroying there is a secret (sirr) that we have not discovered, or a purpose (gharaḍ) that we cannot attain except through it, then let it be posited in our destruction likewise. And the act, in terms of its essential attributes (ṣifāt nafsiyya), is one; so why is it deemed evil from one agent and good from another?
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage is an argument that divine actions cannot be judged by human moral standards, specifically regarding the question of whether God is obligated to reward or punish. The author begins by framing the discussion: we have assumed the discussion about the rule of moral obligation (taklīf). The question is whether reward and punishment become due from God, given that no benefit or harm accrues to Him from His actions. He then acknowledges the dispute between those who affirm and those who deny that rational judgment is possible before revelation, but states that the real issue is whether God must praise, blame, reward, and punish for an action—something that is hidden from us. He asks: how can we know God's pleasure or displeasure without a truthful informant or an indicative action? Moreover, divine actions cannot be measured by human actions. He gives examples: causing pain to the innocent, destroying crops and progeny—these are evil from us but not from God. Similarly, drowning someone is good from God but evil from us. If one argues that there is a hidden purpose behind God's acts that we do not know, then we could similarly posit hidden purposes behind our own destructive acts. The act, considered in its essential attributes, is the same. So why is it evil from one agent and good from another? This shows the fallacy of analogizing divine actions to human actions in determining moral value.

A) Because God is not subject to reward and punishment.

C) Because human reason cannot understand God's actions at all.
D) Because the disputants disagree on rational matters.

Key Takeaway
The moral value of an action is not intrinsic but depends on the nature of the agent; what is evil from humans can be good from God.

Obligation Inconceivable for God and Abundance of Blessings
Author negates obligation regarding God and lists His endless blessings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why obligation (wujūb) cannot be attributed to God and how His continuous blessings negate any notion of divine obligation.

Core Idea
God is under no obligation; His blessings are purely gratuitous.

In this passage, the author makes a bold claim: obligation is absolutely inconceivable with respect to God. He then explains that this is because the slave is perpetually immersed in God's blessings, which have no beginning. This refutes any notion that God is bound to reward human actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the first sentence and note that the author states obligation regarding God is utterly inconceivable.
The term 'غير معقول' means 'not rationally conceivable'.
2
Understand the reasoning about deservingness
The author says that God's claim over the slave is not hard to bear—why? Because the slave is always receiving blessings.
This sets up the argument that God's favors remove any basis for obligation.
3
Examine the list of blessings
The passage enumerates blessings from physical form to knowledge and guidance. Notice the variety and that each is a gift.
This list emphasizes the unending nature of God's favors.
4
Reflect on the Quranic verse
The verse from Ibrahim 34 is quoted to show that God's blessings are countless.
This reinforces the idea that blessings are beyond enumeration.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The phrase 'so there is no beginning of bestowal' means that God's giving is not earned or initiated by any human act.
Thus obligation is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : الواجب في حق الله تعالى غير معقول على الإطلاق، والاستحقاق للرب على العبد غير مستحيل حمله فإنه ما من وقت من الأوقات إلا ويتقلب العبد في نعم كثيرة من نعم الله تعالى ابتداء بأجزل المواهب وأفضل العطايا ؛ من حسن الصورة، وكمال الخلقة، وقوام البنية، وإعداد الآلة، وإتمام الأدلة، وتعديل القناة، نعم وما متعه به من أرواح الحياة وفضله به من حياة الأرواح، وما كرمه به من قبول العلم وآدابه ، وهدايته إلى معرفته التي هي أسنى جوائزه وحبايته ﴿ وَإِن تَعُدُّوا نِعْمَتَ اللَّهِ لَا تُحْصُوهَا ﴾ [إبراهيم: ٣٤]، فلا ابتداء الإنعام
Then we say: The obligatory with respect to God Almighty is inconceivable absolutely, and the deservingness of the Lord over the slave is not impossible to bear. For there is no time from times except that the slave turns over in many blessings from the blessings of God Almighty, beginning with the most abundant gifts and the best grants: from beauty of form, perfection of constitution, strength of build, preparation of instruments, completion of proofs, and straightening of the stature; yes, and what He granted him of the spirits of life and favored him with of the life of spirits, and what He honored him with of acceptance of knowledge and its disciplines, and His guidance to knowledge of Him which is the highest of His gifts and His endowment. "And if you count the blessings of God you cannot enumerate them" [Ibrahim 34]; so there is no beginning of bestowal...
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage argues that any sense of obligation (wujūb) in relation to God is rationally inconceivable. The author then explains that the deservingness of God over the slave is not impossible to bear, meaning that the slave is constantly immersed in God's blessings from the start—physical form, intellect, life, knowledge, guidance. The verse from Ibrahim 34 is quoted to emphasize the incalculable nature of these blessings. The phrase 'so there is no beginning of bestowal' suggests that God's giving is gratuitous and not preceded by any claim or obligation on His part. Thus, human acts cannot create a reciprocal obligation on God.

Key Terms
واجب
استحقاق
نعم
ابتدا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God is obligated to reward human acts of obedience.
Correction: The author stresses that obligation is inconceivable regarding God because His blessings are purely gratuitous and precede any human act.

Checkpoint
What is the author's claim about obligation (wājib) with respect to God?
A) It is rationally necessary.
B) It is inconceivable absolutely.
C) It is established by revelation.
D) It is based on the slave's deservingness.

Key Takeaway
God's blessings are infinite and precede all human acts, making any concept of divine obligation impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the countless blessings you enjoy—from your physical form to your ability to know God—do you genuinely feel that God owes you nothing? How would your daily gratitude change if you truly internalized that these are purely gratuitous gifts, not rewards for your efforts? What specific blessing will you thank God for right now, without linking it to anything you have done?
?
The author states that obligation on God is 'inconceivable absolutely.' Does your heart accept this, or do you sometimes feel entitled to God's mercy or reward because of your worship? Examine your expectations in prayer: do you pray with a sense of debt-collecting or with humble gratitude? Commit to praying one salat today as if you are receiving a gift you could never deserve.
?
The passage lists blessings like 'guidance to knowledge of Him' as the highest gift. Have you ever viewed your faith and knowledge of God as the greatest unearned favour? If so, how does that reshape your approach to seeking more knowledge? Make a concrete plan to spend time this week studying God's names or attributes, not to gain merit, but to appreciate the gift of knowing Him.

God's Actions Have No Cause
Ash'arites state that God creates without any cause or purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'arite position that God's actions are not caused by any external or internal motive, and why this is essential to divine transcendence.

Core Idea
God acts without cause because He needs nothing and is compelled by nothing.

This lesson presents a foundational Ash'arite doctrine: God's creative act is not motivated by any cause or purpose. The source passage from Nihayat al-Aqdam explains why God cannot be influenced by benefit, harm, or any external factor. You will see how this doctrine safeguards God's absolute sovereignty and freedom.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once to get its rhythm. Then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms: علة, غرض, باعث, حامل.
The passage is concise and dense.
2
Identify the two types of causes denied
The passage denies causes beneficial to God and causes beneficial to creatures. Find where each is mentioned and why it is rejected.
God does not accept benefit or harm, and nothing compels Him.
3
Understand the positive statement
The passage concludes that everything exists by God's making, but His making itself has no cause. This affirms God's absolute independence.
Contrast with created agents who act for reasons.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Some think 'no cause' means arbitrary. The passage shows it means no external need or compulsion, not irrationality.
God's wisdom is inherent, not caused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مذهب أهل الحق أن الله تعالى خلق العالم بما فيه من الجواهر والأعراض وأصناف الخلق والأنواع لا لعلة حاملة له على الفعل، سواء قدرت تلك العلة نافعة له أو غير نافعة ؛ إذ ليس يقبل النفع والضر، أو قدرت تلك العلة نافعة للخلق ؛ إذ ليس يبعثه على الفعل باعث فلا غرض له في أفعاله ولا حامل، بل علة كل شيء صنعه ولا علة لصنعه.
The school of the People of Truth is that God Almighty created the world, including its substances, accidents, kinds of creatures, and species, not for a cause impelling Him to the act, whether that cause is deemed beneficial to Him or not beneficial; because He does not accept benefit or harm, or whether that cause is deemed beneficial to the creatures; because nothing motivates Him to act, so there is no purpose in His acts and no impeller. Rather, the cause of everything is His making, and there is no cause for His making.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage defines the Ash'arite position on divine action. It states that God created the entire world—substances, accidents, all creatures and species—without any cause that would impel Him to action. The passage considers two possible types of causes: those that could benefit God Himself, and those that could benefit His creatures. It denies both: God does not receive benefit or harm, so He does not act for His own interest; He also has no motive to act for the benefit of others, because nothing compels Him. Therefore, there is no purpose (gharad) in His actions and no impeller (hāmil). Instead, everything that exists is caused by His making, but His making itself has no cause. This distinguishes God from created agents, who act for reasons.

Key Terms
علة
غرض
باعث
حامل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that saying 'God acts without cause' means His actions are arbitrary or irrational.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'no cause' means no external need or compulsion, not that God acts without wisdom. His actions are perfect and wise, but that wisdom is not a cause that compels Him.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Haqq, why does God not act for a cause?
A) Because He does not know what is beneficial.
B) Because He does not accept benefit or harm and has no impeller.
C) Because He only acts through intermediaries.
D) Because He is compelled by His essence.

Key Takeaway
God's creative act is without any cause, purpose, or motive, affirming His absolute transcendence and freedom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray and ask God for something, do you ever doubt that He will respond because you cannot see a reason for Him to do so? How does the truth that God acts without cause change your confidence in His response to your du'a?
?
Reflect on a time when you felt that God 'owed' you an explanation for a trial. Are you willing to accept that God's actions have no cause that you can comprehend, and that He is not compelled to justify Himself to you?
?
Do you often base your obedience to God on feeling motivated or seeing immediate benefit? How does knowing that God Himself acts without any need or benefit challenge you to worship Him purely for His sake, without expecting anything in return?

True Generosity: Giving Without Motive or Compensation
Defines true generosity as giving without compensation or purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of true generosity and beneficence as giving without any compensation or purpose, and how this applies to God.

Core Idea
True generosity requires zero motive; any purpose makes it an exchange.

This passage from the Kalam tradition draws a sharp line between genuine generosity and forms of giving that are really exchanges. The author argues that any act of giving done for a reward, praise, or even virtue is not true generosity. This teaching prepares us to understand God's absolute transcendence — His giving is without any need or purpose.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and classification
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the first sentence divides giving into categories based on whether it involves a benefit to the giver.
The classification is key to the argument.
2
Understand the subdivisions of beneficial giving
The passage splits benefits into equal (like money-for-money) and unequal (giving for reward, praise, etc.). Both are still forms of exchange.
3
Grasp the positive definition of true generosity
True generosity is defined as bestowing what is appropriate without any compensation or purpose. This is the core definition.
The term 'what is appropriate' (mā yanbaghī) hints at wisdom.
4
Apply to God and contrast with humans
The passage applies this to God, stating He gives perfectly without holding back, and any different giving would be deficient. Then it contrasts with human giving which is motivated by need or desire.
This shows God's transcendence.
5
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why this matters: if God's actions had any motive or purpose, He would depend on something external, contradicting His perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : إفادة الخير والصلاح من المنعم تنقسم إلى ما يكون لفائدة وغرض يرجع إلى المفيد، والفائدة تنقسم إلى ما هو مثل المبذول كمقابلة المال بالمال، وإلى ما ليس مثلا كمن يبذل المال رجاء للثواب والمحمدة أو اكتساب صفة الفضيلة وطلب الكمال به، وهذه أيضًا معاوضة وليس بجود، كما أن الأول معاملة وليس بإنعام بل الجود والإنعام هو إفادة ما ينبغي من غير عوض وغرض، فالأول قد أفاد الجود على الموجودات كلها كما ينبغي على ما ينبغي من غير ادخار ممكن من ضرورة أو حاجة أو رقبة ؛ بحيث لو قدر غيره من الممكنات الوهمية كان نقصا في ذلك الموضوع لا كمالا ، فكل ذلك بلا عوض ولا فائدة، وغيره يتصور أمراً ثم يعرض له احتياج إلى وجوده فيحتال لوجوده ليتم غرضه به ؛ كمن يريد يبني دارا تصورها أولا ثم يحتاج إليها للاستكنان والسكنى احتال لوجودها آلاتها ليتم غرضه بها، أو من يهب مالا أو يعطي عطاء ابتداء أو عقيب سؤال يتصور في نفسه أولا اكتساب حمد وجزاء، وحمله على ذلك ضعف حال الفقير فرق له رقة
They said: The bestowal of good and benefit from the benefactor is divided into what is for a benefit and purpose returning to the benefactor. And benefit is divided into what is equal to that which is given, like exchanging money for money, and into what is not equal, like one who gives money hoping for reward and praise or acquiring the quality of virtue and seeking perfection through it. These are also an exchange and are not generosity, just as the first is a transaction and not beneficence. Rather, generosity and beneficence are bestowing what is appropriate, without compensation and purpose. So the First (i.e., God) has bestowed generosity upon all existents as is appropriate and in the manner that is appropriate, without holding back any possible necessity, need, or watchfulness, such that if another from the imaginary possibles were supposed, it would be a deficiency in that subject, not a perfection. So all that is without compensation and without benefit. Others conceive a thing, then a need for its existence occurs to them, so they contrive for its existence to complete their purpose by it; like one who wants to build a house: he first conceives it, then needs it for shelter and dwelling, so he contrives for its existence and its tools to complete his purpose through it. Or one who gives wealth or gives a gift initially or in response to a request: he first conceives in himself acquiring praise and reward, and what carries him to that is the weakness of the poor person's state, he feels compassion for him.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition of true generosity (jūd) and beneficence (in'ām). It begins by classifying acts of bestowing good into two categories: those that involve a benefit or purpose returning to the giver, and those that do not. Benefits are further subdivided into (a) equivalent compensation, like money for money, and (b) non-equivalent but still benefits, such as giving to gain reward, praise, virtue, or perfection. All such acts are actually forms of exchange (mu'āwaḍa) or transaction (mu'āmala), not true generosity. True generosity is bestowing what is appropriate (mā yanbaghī) without any compensation or purpose. The passage then applies this to God (al-Awwal): He bestows generosity on all existents perfectly, without holding back any necessity or possibility; any supposed different giving would be deficient. Finally, it contrasts this with human giving, which is often motivated by a prior need or desire—like building a house for shelter, or giving to obtain praise or reward, driven by compassion for the poor. This distinction emphasizes God's absolute transcendence of any need or motivation.

Key Terms
جود
إنعام
عوض
غرض
مبذول
معاوضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that giving for a good cause (like charity for reward) is true generosity.
Correction: The passage clarifies that any motive or compensation, even seeking virtue, makes it an exchange, not true generosity.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is true generosity?
A) Giving a lot, even if for reward.
B) Giving what is beneficial to others.
C) Giving without any motive or compensation.
D) Giving equally to all.

Key Takeaway
True generosity is giving without any compensation or purpose, which only applies fully to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of the most selfless act you believe you have ever performed. Can you identify any hidden motive—be it a desire for self-esteem, approval from others, or even a sense of spiritual reward? How does the definition in this passage challenge the purity of that act?
?
The passage states that God's generosity is without any purpose or compensation. Do you ever subconsciously think of God's blessings as 'earned' through your obedience? How does this truth about divine generosity expose any sense of entitlement in your heart?
?
If only God is truly generous, then every time you give, you are essentially admitting your own neediness. How does this realization affect your pride? Will you today consciously perform an act of giving without any expectation—even the expectation of feeling good—and confess your dependence on God's perfect generosity?

The People of Truth Refute Divine Purposive Action
Ahl al-Haqq prove God's absolute independence and discuss relative good/evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq) argue that God must be absolutely independent and that no purpose or motive can be attributed to Him, based on the relativity of good and evil.

Core Idea
God's absolute independence means He is not compelled by any need or purpose.

In this passage, the Sunni theologians (Ahl al-Haqq) directly confront the philosophical claim that divine action must be purposive. They establish the foundational principle of God's absolute independence (al-ghina), showing that any form of need or motive would contradict His perfection. Then they introduce the relativity of good and evil to demonstrate that apparent purposes are not absolute. This lesson will walk through their proof and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the argument's flow.
The passage is truncated at the end; focus on the complete portion.
2
Identify the proof of God's independence
The passage begins with a conditional argument: if God were needy, He would not be the ultimate object of needs. Find and understand this reasoning.
The phrase 'غني عن الإطلاق' is the key concept.
3
Analyze the example of poison
The passage uses poison to show that what is good for one (poison sellers) is evil for another. This illustrates the relativity of good and evil.
This supports the claim that no absolute good can ground divine purpose.
4
Connect to the overall argument
Reflect on how the relativity of good and evil undermines the idea that God creates for a purpose or motive. The passage concludes that we do not deny God's actions are wise (though the text cuts off).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقال أهل الحق: الدليل على أن الباري تعالى غني عن الإطلاق؛ إذ لو كان كان محتاجًا من وجه من ذلك الوجه مفتقرًا إلى من يزيل حاجته، فهو منتهى مطلب الحاجات، ومن عنده نيل الطلبات ولا يبيع نعمه بالأثمان، ولا يكدر عطاياه بالامتنان، فلو خلق شيئًا ما لعلة تحمله على ذلك، أو لداعية تدعوه إليه، أو لكمال يكسبه، أو حمد وأجر يحصله؛ لم يكن غنياً حميداً مطلقا، ولا برا جوادًا مطلقاً، بل كان فقيراً محتاجاً إلى كسب.
والذي يقرره أن كل صلاح نقدره بالعقل بالنسبة إلى شخص عارضه صلاح فوق ذلك، أو فساد مثل ذلك بالنسبة إلى شخص آخر، فلئن كان الصلاح يقتضي وجوده بالنسبة إلى ذلك الشخص فالفساد يقتضي عدمه بالنسبة إلى شخص آخر، فالسم في أصحاب السموم صلاح، وفي غيرهم من الحيوانات فساد، فلو كان الصلاح اقتضى وجوده فالفساد اقتضى عدمه، وكما يختلف ذلك بالأشخاص يختلف الصلاح والفساد بالجزئي والكلي، ونحن لا ننكر أن أفعال الله تعالى
The people of truth said: The proof that the Creator, exalted, is absolutely independent is that if He were in any way needy, dependent on someone to remove His need, (then He would not be the ultimate object of needs). He is the ultimate object of needs, and from Him is the attainment of requests. He does not sell His blessings for prices, nor does He taint His gifts by reminders of favors. So if He created anything for a cause that impelled Him to that, or for a motive that called Him to it, or for a perfection He would acquire, or for praise and reward He would obtain, He would not be absolutely independent, praised, nor truly good and generous; rather, He would be poor and needy for gain. And what he establishes is that every good we conceive by reason relative to a person is opposed by a good above that, or an evil like that relative to another person. For if the good necessitates its existence relative to that person, the evil necessitates its non-existence relative to another person. Poison is good for poison sellers and evil for other animals. So if the good necessitated its existence, the evil necessitated its non-existence. And just as that differs by persons, good and evil differ by the particular and the universal. And we do not deny that the actions of God, exalted,
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage is the response of Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth, i.e., Sunni theologians) to the philosophers' argument about divine generosity. They begin by stating the proof that God is absolutely independent (غني عن الإطلاق). If He were in any way needy, He would depend on something to remove His need, but He is the ultimate goal of all needs. He does not sell His blessings or taint them by reminders. Therefore, if He created for any cause, motive, perfection, or reward, He would not be absolutely independent and praised, but would be poor and needy. Then they establish that every good we conceive is relative: every good for one person is opposed by a greater good or an equal evil for another. For example, poison is good for poison sellers but evil for other animals. So if good necessitated existence, evil would necessitate non-existence. Good and evil differ by persons and by particular and universal. They affirm that they do not deny that God's actions (are wise, etc.—text cuts off).

غني عن الإطلاق
صلاح
فساد
الجزئي والكلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's absolute independence means He cannot have any purpose or wisdom in His actions.
Correction: The argument is that God is not compelled by need or external motive; He may still act wisely, but not for a purpose that fulfills a lack. The passage affirms that we do not deny that His actions are wise (text cut off).

Checkpoint
According to the people of truth, what would it imply if God created for a cause or motive?
A) He would be wise.
B) He would be needy.
C) He would be generous.
D) He would be eternal.

Key Takeaway
God's absolute independence (ghina) and the relativity of good and evil together refute the idea that divine actions are motivated by purpose or need.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

After reading this lesson, how does the relativity of good and evil challenge that assumption? Can you now surrender more fully to His will, even when you don't see the good?
?
If God does not act for any purpose or reward, then what is the ultimate basis for morality and ethics in your life? Are you living to please God simply because He is God, or are you still trying to bargain with Him? What would it mean for you to serve Him with no expectation of return?

God's Absolute Independence: The Proof from Ahl al-Haqq
Ahl al-Haqq prove that God is absolutely independent and acts without any motive or benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot act for any motive or benefit, based on the argument of Ahl al-Haqq that distinguishes between good as a necessary consequence and good as an impelling cause.

Core Idea
God acts without any cause; goodness follows His actions, it does not cause them.

Ahl al-Haqq (the People of Truth) argue convincingly that Allah is absolutely independent, meaning He does not act for any reason, motive, or benefit. This lesson unpacks their proof, starting with the absurdity of God needing anything, and culminating in a crucial distinction between good as a necessary outcome and good as a motive. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping Islamic theology on God's independence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the complete Arabic passage from the source. Then read the literal English translation. Let the author's own argument flow through you before diving into analysis.
Pay attention to the structure: a thesis, a conditional argument, and a final distinction.
2
Identify the main claim and the contrast
Notice that Ahl al-Haqq first state the thesis (God is independent absolutely). Then they argue by contradiction: if God were in need, or acted for any motive (cause, incentive, perfection, reward), He would not be truly independent. Identify these possible motives and understand why they would imply neediness.
3
Examine the example of poison
The passage uses the example of poison: good for poisoners, evil for others. This shows that benefit and harm are relative. Reflect on how this undermines the idea that God acts for the sake of some absolute benefit.
Consider: if benefit were an absolute motive, poison would either always be created or never, but it exists for some and harms others.
4
Understand the final distinction
The passage concludes with a crucial distinction: between good that necessarily attaches to the outcomes of actions (لزوم الخير) and good that impels the actions (حمل الخير). Similarly, there is a distinction between perfection that is an inherent attribute and perfection that is a causal factor. Grasp this distinction—it is the core of the proof.
This distinction shows that God's actions are not motivated by good; rather, good is a necessary result.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقال أهل الحق: الدليل على أن الباري تعالى غني عن الإطلاق؛ إذ لو كان محتاجًا من وجه من ذلك الوجه مفتقرًا إلى من يزيل حاجته، فهو منتهى مطلب الحاجات، ومن عنده نيل الطلبات ولا يبيع نعمه بالأثمان، ولا يكدر عطاياه بالامتنان، فلو خلق شيئًا ما لعلة تحمله على ذلك، أو لداعية تدعوه إليه، أو لكمال يكسبه، أو حمد وأجر يحصله؛ لم يكن غنياً حميداً مطلقا، ولا برا جوادًا مطلقاً، بل كان فقيراً محتاجاً إلى كسب. والذي يقرره أن كل صلاح نقدره بالعقل بالنسبة إلى شخص عارضه صلاح فوق ذلك، أو فساد مثل ذلك بالنسبة إلى شخص آخر، فلئن كان الصلاح يقتضي وجوده بالنسبة إلى ذلك الشخص فالفساد يقتضي عدمه بالنسبة إلى شخص آخر، فالسم في أصحاب السموم صلاح، وفي غيرهم من الحيوانات فساد، فلو كان الصلاح اقتضى وجوده فالفساد اقتضى عدمه، وكما يختلف ذلك بالأشخاص يختلف الصلاح والفساد بالجزئي والكلي، ونحن لا ننكر أن أفعال الله تعالى اشتملت على خير وتوجهت إلى صلاح، وأنه لم يخلق الخلق لأجل الفساد، ولكن الكلام إنما وقع في أن الحامل له على الفعل ما كان صلاحًا يرتقبه وخيرا يتوقعه، بل لا حامل له، وفرق بين لزوم الخير والصلاح لأوضاع الأفعال وبين حمل الخير والصلاح على وضع الأفعال، كما يفرق فرقًا ضروريًا بين الكمال الذي يلزم وجود الشيء، وبين الكمال الذي يستدعي وجود الشيء، فإن الأول فضيلة هي كالصفة اللازمة، والثاني فضيلة كالعلة الحاملة.
The People of Truth said: The proof that the Creator, exalted is He, is independent absolutely: for if He were in need in any respect, dependent on one who removes his need, then He would be the ultimate object of needs, and from Him is the attainment of requests. He does not sell His blessings for prices, nor does He taint His gifts with obligation. So if He created something for a cause that impels Him to that, or for an incentive that calls Him to it, or for a perfection He acquires, or for praise and reward He obtains, He would not be absolutely independent and praiseworthy, nor absolutely benevolent and generous, but rather would be poor and needy for gain. And what he establishes is that every good (salah) we rationally conceive relative to a person is opposed by a good above that, or an evil (fasad) similar to that relative to another person. So if the good necessitates its existence relative to that person, the evil necessitates its non-existence relative to another person. Thus poison is good for poisoners, and for other animals it is evil. So if good necessitated its existence, evil would necessitate its non-existence. And just as this varies by persons, good and evil vary by particular and universal. We do not deny that God's actions include good and are directed toward good, and that He did not create creation for evil; but the discussion is only about whether the impeller for Him to act was a good He anticipated or a benefit He expected, rather He has no impeller. And there is a distinction between the necessary attachment of good and benefit to the circumstances of actions and between the impelling of good and benefit to the establishment of actions, just as there is a necessary distinction between the perfection that is necessary to the existence of a thing and the perfection that requires the existence of a thing. For the first is a virtue like an inherent attribute, and the second is a virtue like a causal impeller.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage is a proof by Ahl al-Haqq (the People of Truth) demonstrating that God is absolutely independent (ghani) and does not act for any motive or cause. The argument proceeds in several steps. First, it states the thesis: God is independent absolutely. Then it argues by contradiction: if God were in need in any way—needing someone to remove his need—then He would be the ultimate object of needs, not the ultimate provider. Next, it lists possible motives for action: a cause, an incentive, a perfection to be gained, or praise and reward. If God acted for any of these, He would not be truly independent and praiseworthy, but rather needy and seeking gain. The proof then addresses the notion of benefit (salah) and harm (fasad). It points out that what is beneficial for one person may be harmful for another, using the example of poison. Therefore, benefit and harm are relative. Since God's actions are not subject to such relativity, He does not act for the sake of benefit. The passage clarifies that while God's actions do contain good and are not aimed at harm, the question is whether a benefit or good anticipated by God impels Him to act. The answer is no: He has no impeller. Finally, a crucial distinction is made: between good necessarily attaching to the outcomes of actions (which is true) and good being the impelling cause for those actions (which is false). This is compared to the distinction between a perfection that necessarily accompanies a thing's existence (like an inherent attribute) and a perfection that causes the thing's existence (like a causal factor). The former is a necessary attribute, the latter is a motive—and God has no motives.

Key Terms
غني
إطلاق
صلاح
فساد
حامل
كمال لازم
كمال مستدعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that since God's actions are good, God must act for the sake of that good.
Correction: Ahl al-Haqq argue that goodness is a necessary consequence of God's actions, not a motive. God acts without any impelling cause; the good follows necessarily.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Haqq in this passage, what is the key distinction regarding goodness in relation to God's actions?
A) Between good that is necessary and good that is contingent
B) Between good that is inherent to actions and good that is accidental
C) Between good that necessarily attaches to the outcomes of actions and good that impels the actions
D) Between good for the agent and good for others

Key Takeaway
God's actions are not motivated by any benefit; rather, benefit necessarily follows His actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself thinking that God must act in a certain way to gain something from you, such as your worship? How does this lesson challenge that assumption and reshape your understanding of why you worship?
?
When you perform good deeds, do you catch yourself expecting a specific return from God, as if He owes you? How can the truth that God is absolutely independent free you from transactional expectations and deepen your devotion?
?
The lesson says good necessarily follows God's actions, not the other way around. In your life, are you content with God's decree even when you cannot see the good in it? What would change if you truly believed that His actions are inherently good, regardless of your perspective?

Refuting the Objection that God Acts for an Aim
The author questions the definition of 'aim' and refutes both self-benefit and other-benefit interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the notion that God acts for an aim (like benefit or preventing harm) is incompatible with divine transcendence and why appeals to benefiting others fail logically.

Core Idea
God's actions are not for personal gain or dependent on others' choices.

In this dialectical passage, the Ash‘ari theologian confronts a common objection: that God must have an aim (gharaḍ) when He acts. The author dissects this claim by examining what 'aim' could mean—benefit to God or benefit to others. He shows that both interpretations lead to contradictions or absurdities, thereby preserving God's absolute transcendence and self-sufficiency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the dialectical structure: objection, questioning of definitions, and response.
Notice the use of rhetorical questions.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'aim'
The author considers two interpretations: benefit to self (jalb naf‘ or daf‘ ḍarar) and benefit to others. Locate where each is discussed.
3
Analyze the refutation of self-benefit
See why self-benefit is impossible: God is transcendent over harm, benefit, pain, and pleasure. He is not a locus for accidents.
This grounds the argument in divine attributes.
4
Examine the refutation of other-benefit
The author questions what absolute benefit is and then shows that the specified aim (inferring God's oneness) is not fully realized. Moreover, the aim depends on others' choice, so it is not guaranteed.
Note the logical point about dependence on free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولكم : لا بد وأن ينحو غرضا ويريد صلاحًا، فما المعنى بالغرض؟ وما المعنى بالصلاح ؟ ولا يشك أنكم تفسرون الغرض باجتلاب نفع أو دفع ضرر ؛ إذ القادر الحق على كل شيء، والغني المطلق عن كل شيء متقدس عن الضرر والنفع والألم واللذة، ويتعالى عن أن يكون محلا للحوادث قابلا للاستحالة والتغيرات، وإن فسرتموه بنفع الغير وصلاحه فما النفع المطلق؟ وما الصلاح المطلق في خلق العالم بأسره ؟ إن قلتم : يستدل بوجود دلائله على وحدانيته. قيل لكم : والحكيم إذا فعل فعلا لغرض معين وجب أن يحصل له ذلك الغرض من كل وجه، ولا يتخلف غرضه من وجه وإلا فينسب إلى الجهل والعجز ومن المعلوم أن الغرض الذي عينتموه لم يحصل إذا قدرتم خلق العالم في الأقل من العقلاء، وإن لم يقرر ذلك لم يحصل في الأكثر، والغرض إذا كان معلقا على اختيار الغير لم يصف عن شوائب الخلاف فلا يحصل لا عقلا ولا سمعاً، ثم لو خلقهم ولم يكلفهم ليفعلوا ما أرادوا يتضرر بذلك، أم يلحقه نقص أو يثلم جلاله فعل، أوليست
And your saying: He must pursue an aim and intend benefit. So what is meant by 'aim'? And what is meant by 'benefit'? And there is no doubt that you interpret 'aim' as bringing benefit or repelling harm. For the True Powerful over all things, the Absolutely Self-sufficient from all things, is sanctified from harm, benefit, pain, and pleasure, and exalted from being a locus for accidents or subject to change and alterations. And if you interpret it as benefit to others and their good, then what is absolute benefit? And what is absolute good in creating the entire world? If you say: to infer by the existence of His proofs His oneness. It was said to you: The wise one, when he does an action for a specific aim, it is necessary that that aim be realized completely; His aim cannot be unfulfilled from any aspect, otherwise He would be attributed to ignorance and incapacity. It is known that the aim you specified has not been realized if you consider the creation of the world with the fewest of the intelligent. And if that is not established, it is not realized in the majority. And the aim, if it is contingent on the choice of another, is not free from the taints of disagreement, so it is not realized neither rationally nor traditionally. Then if He created them and did not obligate them to do what they wanted, would He be harmed by that? Or would harm befall Him or would His majesty be impaired? Or are they not...
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage continues the dialectical exchange. The opponents argue that God must have an aim (gharaḍ) and intend benefit (ṣalāḥ). The author questions their definitions: if they mean benefit to oneself (jalb nafʿ or dafʿ ḍarar), that is impossible for God, who is transcendent over all harm and benefit. If they mean benefit to others, then what is the absolute benefit in creating the entire world? If they say it is to infer His oneness from His proofs, the author responds with a logical objection: if a wise agent acts for a specific aim, that aim must be fully realized. Here, the aim (inference of oneness) is not fully realized because not all intelligent beings infer correctly; in fact, the atheists and ignorant ones fail. Moreover, the aim depends on the free will of others, so it is subject to disagreement and cannot be guaranteed to be realized either rationally or by revelation. The author also raises a further point: if God created humans without obligation, they would do as they please—would that harm God or diminish His majesty? The passage is cut off but clearly aims to show that the objectors' notion of aim leads to absurdities.

الغني المطلق
محلا للحوادث
اختيار الغير
شوائب الخلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God must have a purpose like humans, such as benefiting Himself or others in a way that is fully realized.
Correction: God's actions are not for personal gain nor dependent on contingent choices of creatures; His wisdom transcends human analogies, and any proposed aim like 'being known' is not fully realized.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what problem arises if God's aim is to benefit others (e.g., to be known)?
A) It is impossible because God cannot benefit others
B) The aim is not fully realized because not all know God
C) It makes God dependent on others' free will
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The concept of God acting for an aim like a human is logically untenable because it either implies imperfection in God or fails to achieve its goal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something goes wrong in your life, do you secretly believe God failed to achieve His aim? How does this lesson expose that assumption as a projection of human motives onto the Divine? What would it take for you to truly trust that His actions are beyond your understanding of purpose?
?
The author argues that an aim dependent on others' choices is never fully realized. In what ways do you live as if God's plans depend on your obedience or the obedience of others? How can you surrender that false sense of control today?
?
Think of a time you felt God was unfair because your prayer wasn't answered as you expected. According to this lesson, your expectation was based on imagining God acting with a human-like aim. Will you repent from that assumption and submit to His transcendence, even when you don't understand?

Creation's purpose: beyond reward and pleasure
Objection about obligation and refutation of reward-based teleology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that reducing God's wisdom to human-like purposes, such as reward and pleasure, is inconsistent with divine transcendence, and that the ultimate purpose of creation is beyond such categories.

Core Idea
Reducing God's wisdom to reward-based pleasure is to confine the infinite within human limitations.

This passage presents an objection about why humans are obligated while animals are not, and a response that claims obligation leads to greater pleasure from earned reward. The author then critiques this response, arguing that it confines God's infinite wisdom to a base, human-like purpose. It challenges us to think about the ultimate purpose of creation beyond reward and punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the opening lines that ask why humans are obligated but animals are not, and the response that says obligation allows reward and greater pleasure. Notice the author's critical tone.
2
Follow the chain of purposes
The author outlines a chain: creation → inference → knowledge → obligation of reward → distinction of pleasures. Identify each link and consider why the author finds it objectionable.
This chain is meant to show the absurdity of reducing God's purpose to such a base end.
3
Understand the critique
The author argues that this ultimate purpose is unworthy of a rational being and that God cannot be restricted in creating pleasures. He emphasizes that all pleasures are from God.
The critique aims to elevate God's wisdom above human calculations.
4
Reflect on the supplication
The passage ends with a supplication that turns from deeds to God's mercy. Consider how this relates to the argument about purpose.
It shows the spiritual conclusion: reliance on mercy rather than reward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطيور في الهوى والسوائم في الفلا تغدو وتروح من غير تكليف؟ فما السر في تخصيص بني آدم بالتكليف ولم ينتفع به ولا يتضرر بضده؟ قالوا: ليأتي المكلف بما أمر به فيثيبه عليه ويعوضه عما فات عليه، ويكون التذاذ المكلف بالثواب والعوض المرتبين على فعله أكثر من التذاذه بنفس التفضل والكرم. قيل: يا الله العجب من حكمة ما أشرفها، ومن سر أدقه! تعبت عقولكم شدة التعمق في استخراج المعاني، فقد عاد حكمة الله تعالى في خلق السموات والأرض بما فيها إلى أن يكون التذاذ المكلف بثواب يناله على عمله أكثر من التذاذه بتفضل يناله من غير عمل، إنا إذا فحصنا عن الأغراض كان الغرض من خلق العالم هو الاستدلال، وكان الغرض من الاستدلال حصول المعرفة، وكان الغرض من حصول المعرفة وجوب الثواب، وكان الغرض من الثواب حصول التفرقة بين لذتي المقابلة والعطية، فغرض الأغراض من خلق العالم بما فيه من الجواهر والأعراض ما لا يجوز أن يكون غرضا لعاقل، ولا يقدر الخالق على أن يخلق لذة في التفضل أكثر مما يخلقها في الثواب واللذات كلها مخلوقة الله تعالى. أولا ينادي: يا رب مغفرتك أوسع من ذنوبي، ورحمتك أرجى عندي من عملي، يا من لا تنفعه المغفرة ولا تضره المعصية، اغفر لي ما لا يضرك، وأعطني ما لا ينفعك، حتى يكون ابتهاجي برحمتك ومغفرتك ألطف من التذاذي بمعرفتي وطاعتي.
The birds in the air and the grazing livestock in the desert come and go without obligation? So what is the secret in specifying the children of Adam with obligation, while He does not benefit from it nor is harmed by its opposite? They said: So that the obligated one may do what he is commanded, so that He rewards him for it and compensates him for what he missed, and the pleasure of the obligated one with the reward and compensation attached to his action is greater than his pleasure with mere grace and generosity. It was said: O God, how amazing is His wisdom, how fine Its secret! Your intellects have become weary from the intensity of deep investigation in extracting meanings, for the wisdom of God, exalted, in creating the heavens and the earth and what is in them has come down to this: that the pleasure of the obligated one with a reward he attains for his deed is greater than his pleasure with grace he attains without deed. If we investigate the purposes, the purpose of creating the world is inference, the purpose of inference is to obtain knowledge, the purpose of obtaining knowledge is the obligation of reward, and the purpose of reward is to obtain a distinction between the pleasure of recompense and the pleasure of gift. So the ultimate purpose of creating the world, including its substances and accidents, is something that no rational person would consider a purpose, and the Creator cannot create a pleasure in grace greater than that which He creates in reward; all pleasures are created by God. Or does he not call out: O Lord, Your forgiveness is broader than my sins, and Your mercy is more hopeful to me than my deeds, O He whom forgiveness does not benefit nor does disobedience harm, forgive me what does not harm You, and give me what does not benefit You, so that my delight in Your mercy and forgiveness may be more subtle than my pleasure in my knowledge and obedience.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-response argument about the purpose of creation and divine obligation. It begins with an objection: why are humans obligated when birds and livestock are not? The response (attributed to 'they said') is that obligation allows reward and compensation, which bring greater pleasure than unearned grace. The author then criticizes this response, arguing that it reduces God's wisdom to a base human purpose. The critique examines the chain of purposes: creation leads to inference, inference to knowledge, knowledge to obligation of reward, and reward to distinguishing pleasures. This, the author says, is unworthy of a rational being, and God can create any degree of pleasure. The passage ends with a supplication emphasizing reliance on God's mercy rather than deeds.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's wisdom in creation is primarily to give humans a chance to earn reward and that reward-based pleasure is the highest purpose.
Correction: The author argues that this reduces God's wisdom to a base, human-like purpose and that God can create any degree of pleasure directly without need for obligation.

Checkpoint

A) It allows humans to prove their worth.
B) The reward and compensation bring greater pleasure than unearned grace.
C) It fulfills God's need for obedience.
D) It distinguishes humans from animals.

Key Takeaway
The ultimate purpose of creation is not for God's gain, and even our knowledge should lead us to rely on His mercy rather than on our deeds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If God's purpose in creating the universe is not primarily for your happiness or reward, what does that mean for how you approach trials and your sense of entitlement to ease? How can you remind yourself of this when you feel that God 'owes' you something?

The Beggar and the King: Exposing the Absurdity of Reward-Based Divine Wisdom
The author uses an analogy of a beggar and a king to expose the absurdity of rewarding God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy of the beggar and the king exposes the absurdity of claiming that God's wisdom in creation is aimed at rewarding deeds.

Core Idea
Reward-based divine wisdom reduces God to a needy beggar.

This passage employs a powerful analogy to highlight the flaw in a view that ascribes a reward-seeking purpose to God. The author compares such a view to a beggar giving a paltry gift to a generous king and then demanding payment. As we read, we will see how this reductio ad absurdum argues for God's utter transcendence and lack of need.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the scenario of the beggar and king.
Notice the contrast between the beggar's humble gift and the king's boundless generosity.
2
Identify the absurdity
Observe the beggar's demand for compensation despite the king's generosity. This is the core of the reductio.
3
Connect to divine wisdom
Understand how the author applies the analogy to the claim about God's purpose in creation.
The analogy shows that such a claim would place God's wisdom at the lowest level of aspiration.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
See how the analogy reduces the rival view to an unacceptable level, even for mundane repairs.
The final exclamation emphasizes God's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو لا يعد من غاية اللؤم وركاكة الهمة أن يهدي فقير هدية حقيرة إلى ملك كبير سجيته البذل والعطايا من غير سؤال، وعرض هدية لنيل ثواب، ثم يوجب عليه العوض ويقول: التذاذي بما يقابل هديتي أكثر من عطاياك التي لا تحصى، انظر كيف عادت الحكمة الإلهية في خلق العالم بأسره عند القوم إلى أخس الدرجات في الهمة وأمس الحاجات إلى المرمة؛ بحيث لا يرتضيه عاقل لإرمام بيته الكثيف، فكيف يرتضيه الفاطر لأحكام صنعه اللطيف؟! فتعالى وتقدس.
Or is it not counted among the extreme baseness and weakness of aspiration that a poor person gives a humble gift to a great king, whose character is generosity and gifts without being asked, and presents the gift to obtain reward, then obligates compensation and says: my pleasure in what corresponds to my gift is greater than your incalculable gifts? Look how the divine wisdom in creating the whole world, according to these people, has come down to the lowest levels of aspiration and the most pressing need for patching up, such that no rational person would approve it for patching up his dense house, so how would the Creator approve it for the rulings of His subtle craft? Exalted and high is He.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is an reductio ad absurdum argument in the form of an analogy. The author compares the view that God’s purpose in creation is to give reward for deeds to a beggar giving a trivial gift to a generous king, then demanding compensation and claiming his pleasure in the exchange is greater than the king's unasked generosity. This analogy exposes the absurdity of attributing such a base motive to God, concluding that no rational person would accept such reasoning for fixing his own house, let alone for the Creator's perfect craftsmanship.

Correction: The analogy shows this reduces God to a beggar demanding compensation from a generous king, which is absurd.

Key Takeaway
The analogy of the beggar and king demonstrates that attributing a reward-seeking purpose to God is incompatible with His absolute transcendence and generosity.

The lam of consequence and the principle of not questioning God's actions
The author explains Quranic 'lam' and establishes that God's actions are not subject to 'why'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the lam of consequence and the lam of causation in the Quran, and why it is invalid to ask 'why' about God's actions.

Core Idea
God's actions are not subject to 'why' — they are questions of outcome, not cause.

This lesson explores two related points from the author: the correct interpretation of the 'lam' in certain Quranic verses, and the broader principle that God's actions cannot be questioned with 'why'. These points protect God's transcendence and reaffirm that He is not answerable to His creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two examples the author gives and the concluding Quranic verse.
The passage combines a grammatical point with a theological principle.
2
Distinguish the two types of 'lam'
Identify how the author explains the 'lam' in the recompense verse as lam al-ma'al (of ultimate consequence) not lam al-ta'lil (of causation). Reflect on the Pharaoh example.
This shows that God's actions are not motivated by created outcomes.
3
Understand the principle of not questioning God
Read how the author extends the same logic: 'why' does not apply to God's essence, attributes, or actions. Note the list of invalid questions and the final Quranic proof.
The principle is rooted in divine transcendence and sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الآيات في مثل قوله تعالى: وَلِتُجْزَى كُلُّ نَفْسٍ بِمَا كَسَبَتْ فهي لام المآل وصيرورة العاقبة، لا لام التعليل كما قال تعالى: فَالْتَقَطَهُ آلُ فِرْعَوْنَ لِيَكُونَ لَهُمْ عَدُوًا وَحَزَنًا، وقوله: جَعَلَ لَكُمُ اللَّيْلَ لِتَسْكُنُوا فِيهِ وَالنَّهَارَ لِتَبْتَغُوا مِنْ فَضْلِهِ واعلم أنه كما لا تتطرق لم إلى ذات الباري تعالى وصفاته لم تتطرق إلى صنائعه وأفعاله، حتى لا يلزم أن يجاب، لأنه كذا أو لكونه كذا، فلا يقال: لم وجد؟ ولم كان العالم؟ ولا يقال: لم أوجد العالم؟ ولم خلق العباد؟ ولم كلف العقلاء؟ ولم أمر ونهى؟ ولم قدر وقضى؟ لا يُسْأَلُ عَمَّا يَفْعَلُ وَهُمْ يُسْأَلُونَ.
As for the verses like His statement, exalted: 'So that every soul may be recompensed for what it earned' – it is the lam of ultimate consequence and outcome, not the lam of causation, just as He said: 'Then the family of Pharaoh picked him up so that he would become an enemy and a grief to them', and His statement: 'He made for you the night that you may rest therein and the day that you may seek of His bounty.' And know that just as 'why' does not apply to the essence of the Creator, exalted, and His attributes, it does not apply to His works and actions, so that no answer is required because it is so or because it is such. So it is not said: Why did He exist? Why was the world? Nor is it said: Why did He create the world? Why did He create the servants? Why did He obligate the rational? Why did He command and forbid? Why did He decree and ordain? 'He is not questioned about what He does, but they will be questioned.'
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is a hermeneutical and theological clarification. The author distinguishes between two types of 'lam' in Quranic Arabic: the lam of causation (lam al-ta'lil) and the lam of ultimate consequence (lam al-ma'al). He argues that verses like 'that every soul may be recompensed' (التغابن:22) indicate the outcome, not the purpose. He supports this with the example of Pharaoh's family picking Moses unknowingly leading to enmity. Then he establishes a general principle: just as 'why' does not apply to God's essence and attributes, it does not apply to His actions. Therefore, questions like 'Why did He create the world?' are invalid because God is not questioned about His deeds (الأنبياء:23).

Key Terms
لام المآل
لام التعليل
لم
لا يُسْأَلُ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the 'lam' in verses like 'so that every soul may be recompensed' indicates God's purpose or causation for those outcomes, and that one can legitimately ask 'why' about God's actions.
Correction: The 'lam' is actually lam al-ma'al (of ultimate consequence), not causation. And God is not questioned about His actions; He is beyond such interrogation.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the 'lam' in the verse 'So that every soul may be recompensed'?
A) It is a lam of causation (lam al-ta'lil).
B) It is a lam of ultimate consequence (lam al-ma'al).
C) It is a lam of emphasis.
D) It is a lam of exception.

Key Takeaway
The lam in verses about reward indicates ultimate consequence, not divine motivation, and God is never questioned about His actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a calamity befalls you, do you find yourself asking 'why me?'? How does this lesson confront that urge, and what will you do the next time such a question arises?
?
Have you ever tried to justify God's actions to yourself or others, as if He needs a reason acceptable to you? What does the author's argument about the lam of consequence teach you about your tendency to seek explanations? Will you commit to stopping that today?
?
The passage lists questions like 'why did He create the servants?' and 'why did He command and forbid?'. Is there a specific decree in your life that you have mentally questioned? Will you now surrender that question to Him, acknowledging that He is not questioned?

God's Actions Are Beyond 'Why': The Lām of Outcome
Author argues divine actions cannot be questioned with 'why'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine actions cannot be questioned with 'why', and how Quranic verses that seem to indicate purpose actually describe outcomes.

Core Idea
God's actions are not caused; they are final—'why' does not apply.

Many readers of the Quran encounter verses that seem to suggest God acts for a purpose, such as 'that every soul may be recompensed'. This can lead to the assumption that God's actions are motivated by causes. In this passage, the author clarifies a subtle point of Arabic grammar and theological principle: these verses indicate the ultimate outcome, not a motivating reason. He then extends this to all divine actions, grounding his argument in the Quranic verse 'He is not asked about what He does.'

Guided Path
1
Examine the Quranic examples
Read the two Quranic examples given: (ولتجزى كل نفس بما كسبت) and (فالتقطه آل فرعون ليكون لهم عدوا وحزنا). Notice the lām in each.
The second example clearly shows that the lām cannot be causal because Pharaoh's family did not intend to pick up an enemy.
2
Distinguish lam al-ma‘al from lam al-ta‘lil
The author argues the lām in these verses is lam al-ma‘al (final outcome) not lam al-ta‘lil (causation). Understand the difference: causation explains motive, outcome describes result.
3
Apply the principle to God's actions
The author states that just as 'why' does not apply to God's essence and attributes, it does not apply to His actions. Read the list of questions that are forbidden: لم وجد؟ لم كان العالم؟ etc.
These questions are not answerable because they assume a cause for God's actions.
4
Connect to the Quranic conclusion
The passage ends with verse 21:23: 'He is not asked about what He does, but they are asked.' Reflect on how this verse seals the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الآيات في مثل قوله تعالى : ( وَلِتُجْزَى كُلُّ نَفْسٍ بِمَا كَسَبَتْ ) فهي لام المآل وصيرورة العاقبة، لا لام التعليل كما قال تعالى : ( فَالْتَقَطَهُ وَالُ فِرْعَوْنَ لِيَكُونَ لَهُمْ عَدُوا وَحَزَنًا ) وقوله : ( جعل لكم الليل لتسكنوا فيه والنهار ولتبتغوا من فضله ) واعلم أنه كما لا تتطرق لم إلى ذات الباري تعالى وصفاته لم تتطرق إلى صنائعه وأفعاله، حتى لا يلزم أن يجاب، لأنه كذا أو لكونه كذا، فلا يقال: لم وجد؟ ولم كان العالم؟ ولا يقال: لم أوجد العالم؟ ولم خلق العباد؟ ولم كلف العقلاء؟ ولم أمر ونهى؟ ولم قدر وقضى؟ ( لَا يُسْتَلُ عَمَّا يَفْعَلُ وَهُمْ يُسْتَلُونَ ) [الأنبياء : 23].
As for the verses such as His statement the Exalted: 'And that every soul may be recompensed for what it earned' – that is the lām of final outcome and becoming of the consequence, not the lām of causation, just as He said the Exalted: 'And the family of Pharaoh picked him up to be for them an enemy and a sorrow' and His statement: 'He made for you the night that you may rest therein and the day that you may seek of His bounty.' And know that just as 'why' does not apply to the essence of the Exalted God and His attributes, it does not apply to His creatures and His actions, such that it cannot be required to answer, 'because it is so' or 'for being so'. So it is not said: Why did He exist? Why was the world? Nor is it said: Why did He create the world? Why did He create servants? Why did He charge the rational? Why did He command and forbid? Why did He decree and predestine? 'He is not asked about what He does, but they are asked' [Al-Anbiya: 23].
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument defending the transcendent nature of God’s actions against those who claim they must be motivated by purposes or causes. The author begins by interpreting Quranic verses that seem to indicate purpose, such as 'And that every soul may be recompensed for what it earned.' He argues that the lām (preposition 'li') in such verses is not the lām of causation (lam al-ta‘līl) but the lām of final outcome (lam al-ma‘āl) or consequence (ṣīrūrat al-‘āqiba). That is, the verse states the result, not the reason. He provides a parallel from the story of Moses: the family of Pharaoh picked him up 'to be' an enemy and sorrow — clearly not their intention. Likewise, the lām in verses about night and day indicates ultimate outcome. The author then extends the principle: just as we do not ask 'why' about God’s essence or attributes, we should not ask 'why' about His actions. God’s actions are not susceptible to causal questioning; one cannot say 'why did He exist?' or 'why did He create the world?' etc. He concludes with the Quranic verse 'He is not asked about what He does, but they are asked.' This establishes that theological inquiry into God’s actions ends when they are recognized as beyond human interrogation.

Key Terms
لام المآل
لام التعليل
صيرورة العاقبة
لا يسأل عما يفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's actions must have purposes or causes that can be questioned with 'why'.
Correction: Quranic verses that seem to indicate purpose actually describe outcomes, and applying 'why' to God's actions is categorically forbidden.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the lām in 'li-tujzā kullu nafsin' (Q. 64:22) indicate?
A) Causation (al-ta‘līl)
B) Final outcome (al-ma‘āl)
C) Possession
D) Oath

Key Takeaway
God's actions are not subject to 'why' questions; they are beyond human causation, as affirmed by the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face trials that you cannot explain, what does your heart instinctively demand from God? Will you now choose to silence the 'why' and instead ask for the strength to submit?
?
What would it take for you to truly believe that God's actions are not answerable to your sense of fairness? Are you willing to let go of the need to understand His decree in your life?
?
If you are never given an answer to your deepest questions about suffering or divine wisdom, will your trust in God remain? Or has your faith been conditional on receiving explanations?

Third Ash‘ari Objection: Eternal Punishment Not the Most Sound
Ash‘ariyya argue that eternal punishment cannot be the most sound for the damned.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third Ash‘ari objection to the Mu‘tazila principle of al-aṣlaḥ (optimal benefit) regarding the eternal punishment of the damned.

Core Idea
If God must do what is most sound, eternal punishment fails the test.

The Mu‘tazila argued that God must always do what is most beneficial for His creatures, including eternal punishment of the damned which prevents further sin. The Ash‘ariyya object that if that were the criterion, God could simply annihilate the damned or forgive them to achieve even greater benefit. This passage presents that objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the structure: an assertion, a response, and two alternative scenarios.
2
Identify the Mu‘tazila claim being challenged
The passage begins by stating the Mu‘tazila judgment that eternal stay in Hell is 'soundness' (ṣalāḥ) for the damned. Understand this as their application of the principle of al-aṣlaḥ.
The Mu‘tazila also argue that if the damned were returned to the world, they would relapse into sin.
3
Examine the Ash‘ari counter
The Ash‘ariyya propose that if God caused them to die or removed their intellects and stopped punishment, that would be 'more sound'. Similarly, forgiveness and mercy would also be more sound. This undercuts the necessity of eternal punishment.
The objection uses the Mu‘tazila's own criterion against them.
4
Understand the logical implications
The Ash‘ari objection shows that the Mu‘tazila's principle leads to absurdity if applied consistently: eternal punishment is not the most beneficial option available to God.
This refutes the idea that God is obliged to do the most sound for creatures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها القضاء بأن خلود أهل النار في النار يجب أن يكون صلاحا لهم، وقولهم بأنهم لو ردوا لعادوا لما نهوا عنه لا يغني فإن الله تعالى لو أماتهم أو سلب عقولهم وقطع عقابهم كان أصلح لهم، ولو غفر لهم ورحمهم وأخرجهم من النار؛ إذ لم يتضرر بكفرهم وعصيانهم كان أصلح لهم.
And among them the judgment that the eternal stay of the people of Hellfire in the Fire must be soundness for them, and their statement that if they were returned they would return to what they were forbidden does not suffice, for if God, exalted, had caused them to die or removed their intellects and stopped their punishment, it would be more sound for them, and if He had forgiven them and had mercy on them and brought them out of the Fire, since He is not harmed by their unbelief and disobedience, it would be more sound for them.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This objection addresses the Mu‘tazila claim that eternal punishment of the damned is 'sound' (ṣalāḥ) for them. The Ash‘ariyya argue: if it were a requirement for God to do what is most sound for His creatures, then eternal punishment would not be the most sound for the damned. Rather, it would be more sound for them if God caused them to die, or removed their intellects and stopped their punishment, or forgave them and brought them out of the Fire. Since God is not harmed by their unbelief, forgiveness would be more beneficial. The Mu‘tazila might reply that they would return to sin if given a second chance, but the Ash‘ariyya counter that God could annihilate them or remove their intellects. Thus the Mu‘tazila position leads to the absurdity that eternal punishment is not the most sound.

Key Terms
خلود
أهل النار
صلاح
أصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this objection denies the justice of eternal punishment or suggests that all sinners should be forgiven regardless.
Correction: The objection is not a denial of divine justice, but a logical refutation of the Mu‘tazila claim that God is obliged to do what is most beneficial. It points out that if the criterion of optimal benefit were binding, God would have chosen an even more beneficial outcome.

Checkpoint
According to this objection, what would be more sound for the people of Hellfire?
A) Eternal punishment
B) Being annihilated or having their punishment stopped
C) Being given another chance to believe
D) Being kept in a state of ignorance

Key Takeaway
Eternal punishment is not the most sound option for the damned, contrary to Mu‘tazila claims, because God could have chosen even more beneficial alternatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the fate of the damned, do you find yourself leaning toward a view that God must always do what is most beneficial according to human reasoning? What does this lesson reveal about the limits of your own logic before divine wisdom?
?
The passage suggests that forgiveness could be more beneficial than eternal punishment. If you truly believe that God could forgive anyone at any time, how does that affect your sense of urgency in calling others to repentance? Are you living as if God's mercy might override justice?
?
If the Mu'tazila were correct that God must do what is most beneficial, would you feel more secure or more anxious about your own salvation? How does the Ash'ari objection challenge your trust in God's absolute freedom and wisdom?
