The Prophet’s Deferral to God as Proof of Truthfulness
Response to objection that prophet defers to God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the prophet's deferral to God's will is a proof of his truthfulness and how the concept of essential differentia establishes divine infallibility.

Core Idea
The prophet's referral to God is the strongest evidence of his sincerity, not a hesitation.

When skeptics object that the prophet refers matters to God's will, they see it as a sign of uncertainty. However, the author of Nihayat al-Aqdam turns this objection into a powerful proof: the prophet's constant deferral is precisely what confirms he is a genuine messenger. In this passage, he explains that a true prophet never claims independence but shows utter reliance on Allah. This reliance is the mark of divine infallibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the author starts by repeating the objection.
2
Identify the objection and the response
Find the objection: that the prophet defers to God's will. Then see how the author responds by saying this deferral is actually a proof.
3
Understand the reasoning
The author argues that if the prophet claimed independence, he would not be a true messenger. Deferral shows he is acting on behalf of God.
This is a logical refutation of the objection.
4
Analyze the essential differentia
The author introduces the concept of 'essential differentia' for the prophet: he is never left to himself. This ensures he does not speak from desire.
Link this to the Qur'anic verse about not speaking from desire.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that this deference and infallibility prove the prophet's truthfulness. Summarize in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولكم : إن الرسول يحيل نزول الآية إلى مشيئة الله تعالى، قلنا : تلك الحوالة من أدل الأدلة على صدق المقالة ; إذ لو ادعى الاستقلال بإظهار الآيات ما كان مخبرا الله تعالى بأمره، ولا داعيًا إلى الله بإذنه، فهو في كل حال من الأحوال يثبت حق موكله، ويظهر العجز من نفسه ويحيل الحول والقوة إلى مرسله وكان الفصل الذاتي لنفس النبي عليه السلام أن لا يكون موكولا لنفسه طرفة عين، فلا ينطق عن الهوى، ولا يتحرك إلا على متن الهدى، وذلك هو العصمة الإلهية المقيمة لنفسه المقومة لذاته، ولئن كان النطق فصلا ذاتيا لنوع الإنسان ( وما ينطق عن الهوى إن هو إلا وحي يوحى ) فصل ذاتي لنفس النبي عليه السلام، اعرف الإشارة ولا تؤاخذني بالعبارة.
And your saying: The prophet refers the descent of the verse to the will of God Almighty, we said: That referral is among the strongest proofs of the truthfulness of the statement; because if he claimed independence in manifesting the signs, he would not be informing of God Almighty's command, nor would he be calling to God by His permission. Thus in every state he affirms the right of his principal, and shows incapacity from himself, and refers all power and strength to his sender. And the essential differentia of the prophet's self (peace be upon him) is that he is not entrusted to himself for the blink of an eye, so he does not speak from desire, nor does he move except upon the path of guidance. That is the divine infallibility that establishes his self and constitutes his essence. And if speech is an essential differentia for the human species – 'Nor does he speak from desire; it is only a revelation revealed' [An-Najm 53:3-4] – it is an essential differentia for the prophet's self (peace be upon him). Understand the allusion and do not hold me to the expression.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage responds to an objection about the prophet deferring to God's will. The author argues that this referral is actually the strongest evidence of his truthfulness, because if the prophet claimed independence in producing miracles, he would not be acting as a messenger. By referring everything to God, he demonstrates his incapacity and affirms his subordination to God. The author then states that the essential differentia of the prophet is that he is never left to himself, so he never speaks from desire. This is the meaning of divine infallibility. The author uses the Qur'anic verse "Nor does he speak from desire; it is only a revelation revealed" to support this, and asks the reader to understand the allusion.

Key Terms
الحوالة
الاستقلال
العصمة الإلهية
الفصل الذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the prophet's deferral to God indicates uncertainty or lack of authority.
Correction: The author shows that deferral is actually a proof of truthfulness, because a true messenger relies entirely on God.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the prophet's referral of the matter to God's will indicate?
A) His lack of confidence
B) His independence
C) The truthfulness of his message
D) His ignorance

Key Takeaway
The prophet's constant referral to God's will demonstrates his truthfulness and divine infallibility, as he never acts independently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where you are tempted to claim credit or act independently, do you instead refer all power to God like the prophet did? How can you practice this deferral in your daily life to demonstrate sincerity?
?
The prophet never spoke from desire; examine your own speech. How much of what you say is driven by your own desires rather than guidance? What step can you take today to align your speech with truth?
?
The prophet's essential differentia was that he was never entrusted to himself. In what area of your life are you still trusting yourself instead of relying on God's guidance? What would it look like to surrender that area fully to God?

The Error of Limiting I'jaz to Long Suras
Shahristani criticizes restricting i'jaz and demonstrates from the first verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Shahristani refutes the idea that the Quran's miraculous nature is confined to long chapters, using the first revealed verses as a counterexample.

Core Idea
Even the shortest verses can be the most miraculous.

Shahristani opens by criticizing those who restrict the Quran's inimitability (i'jaz) to long suras or specific verses. He immediately directs attention to the very first revelation—Surah al-'Alaq—to show that even these few words are packed with profound eloquence and wisdom. This lesson examines his initial argument and the rhetorical depth he uncovers in the opening verses.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening criticism
Read the first sentence: 'ولقد قصر من قصد إعجاز القرآن في سورة طويلة وحصره في آيات مخصوصة'. Notice how Shahristani dismisses the restriction of i'jaz.
He uses the word 'قصر' (fell short) to indicate inadequacy.
2
Examine the first verse analysis
Read the analysis of Surah al-'Alaq 1-2: 'انظر إلى أولى آية نزلت كيف اشتملت على وجوه من البلاغة والحكم'. Focus on how he claims it contains multiple aspects of eloquence and wisdom.
He asks a rhetorical question to emphasize the impossibility of surpassing this eloquence.
3
Identify the key rhetorical features
Notice the features he highlights: generality and specificity, the name 'Rabb', and the concise yet comprehensive wording.
He says it is 'أفصح منه بيانا وأوجز لفظا وأشرف نظماً وأبلغ عبارة ومعنى'.
4
Understand the purpose
Reflect on why Shahristani chooses the first revelation. It shows that i'jaz is present from the very beginning, not limited to later long suras.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقد قصر من قصد إعجاز القرآن في سورة طويلة وحصره في آيات مخصوصة، انظر إلى أولى آية نزلت كيف اشتملت على وجوه من البلاغة والحكم: {اقرأ باسم ربك الذي خلق، خلق الإنسان من علق} [العلق: 1-2]. فمن أراد أن يعبر عن تقدير الخالقية عموما لجميع الخلق في العالم، ثم خصوصاً للإنسان المطابق خلقته بخلقة العالم بأسره، كيف يعبر عنه بلفظ أفصح منه بياناً وأوجز لفظا وأشرف نظماً وأبلغ عبارة ومعنى. ثم انظر كيف خصص اسم الربوبية في حال تربيته، وكيف عمم الخلق أولا ثم خصص، وكيف ابتدأ خلق الإنسان من العلق حيث كانت مرتبته في حال قبول صورة الوحي مرتبة العلق من قبول صورة الإنسان إلى أن انتهى إلى البلاغ السابع من قبول جميع القرآن: {الرحمن، علم القرآن، خلق الإنسان، علمه البيان} [الرحمن: 1-4] كما ينتهي إلى المرتبة السابعة حتى يقبل علم البيان. وكيف قرن {اقرأ وربك الأكرم، الذي علم بالقلم، علم الإنسان ما لم يعلم} [العلق: 3-5]. فالحكمة في اقرأ أولا وثانيا {وربك الأكرم} أبلغ لفظا ومعنى من {اقرأ باسم ربك}، ثم عمم التعلم ثم خصص الإنسان، كما عمم الخلق ثم خصص الإنسان. وكيف اجتمعت الفصاحة في ألفاظ بإشعار وجوه المعاني، وكيف اقترنت بهذه الجزالة على قلة حروفها وخفة كلماتها من غير إخلال في الدلالة، وكيف انتظمت بأشرف تركيب من حيث اللفظ، وأصح ترتيب من حيث المعنى، وكيف لاحت فيها البلاغة برطوبة الألفاظ ومتانة المعاني حتى كأنها جمعت علم الأولين والآخرين من تقرير العموم والخصوص، وتقدم الأمر على الخلق، وإضافة الهداية والخلق إليه {تبارك الله رب العالمين} [غافر: 64]. فتبين من هذه القرائن أن القرآن بجملته معجز، وذلك في حق من لا يعرف دقائق البلاغة وحقائق المعاني، وبعشر سور منه معجز في حق قوم، وبسورة واحدة معجز في حق قوم، وبآية واحدة معجز في حق قوم، وتتقدر مقادير الإعجاز على قدر الدراية والتحقيق.
And indeed, those who restricted the inimitability (i'jaz) of the Qur'an to a long sura and confined it to specific verses have fallen short. Look at the first verse that was revealed, how it contained aspects of eloquence (balaghah) and wisdom (hikam): {Read in the name of your Lord who created, created man from a clot} [al-'Alaq 96:1-2]. For whoever wants to express the estimation of creatorship generally for all creation in the world, then specifically for man whose creation corresponds to the creation of the whole world, how would he express it with wording more eloquent (afsah) in exposition, more concise in expression, more noble in composition, and more forceful in phrase and meaning? Then look how He specified the name of Lordship (rububiyyah) in the state of His nurturing (tarbiyah), and how He generalized creation first then specified, and how He began the creation of man from a clot when his station, in the state of receiving the form of revelation, was the station of the clot from receiving the form of man until he reached the seventh communication (balagh) of receiving the entire Qur'an: {The Most Merciful, taught the Qur'an, created man, taught him eloquence} [al-Rahman 55:1-4] as he reaches the seventh rank until he receives the science of eloquence (bayan). And how He linked {Read, and your Lord is the Most Generous, who taught by the pen, taught man what he did not know} [al-'Alaq 96:3-5]. So the wisdom in 'Read' first and second, and {your Lord is the Most Generous} is more eloquent in wording and meaning than {Read in the name of your Lord}, then He generalized learning then specified man, just as He generalized creation then specified man. And how eloquence (fasahah) gathered in words with indication of aspects of meanings, and how it was accompanied by this grandeur (jazalah) despite the fewness of its letters and lightness of its words without deficiency in indication, and how it was organized in the most noble composition in terms of wording, and the soundest arrangement in terms of meaning, and how eloquence (balaghah) appeared in it with freshness of words and solidity of meanings until it seemed to have gathered the knowledge of the earlier and later generations from the establishment of generality and specificity, and the precedence of command over creation, and the attribution of guidance and creation to Him {Blessed is Allah, Lord of the worlds} [Ghafir 40:64]. So it becomes clear from these indicators that the entire Qur'an is miraculous (mu'jiz), and that is for those who do not know the subtleties of eloquence and the realities of meanings; and by ten suras of it it is miraculous for some people; and by one sura it is miraculous for some people; and by one verse it is miraculous for some people. And the measures of inimitability (i'jaz) are estimated according to the measure of knowledge (dirayah) and verification (tahqiq).
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage is an argument demonstrating the inimitability (i'jaz) of the Qur'an, responding to those who restrict it to long suras. The author (Shahristani) begins by criticizing those who limit the Qur'an's miraculous nature to long suras or specific verses, asserting that they have fallen short. He then directs attention to the very first revealed verse (Surah al-'Alaq, 96:1-2) as a counterexample. He analyzes its eloquence in several aspects: first, if one wanted to express God's creativity both general and specific (the whole world and man as a microcosm), no wording could be more eloquent and concise. Second, he observes the specification of the name 'Lord' (al-Rabb) in the context of tarbiyah (nurturing), the generalization of creation followed by specification of man, and the beginning of man's creation from a clot—likening the stages of revelation (the seven stations of receiving the Qur'an) to the stages of human development. Third, he notes the parallelism with Surah al-Rahman, where the sequence 'taught the Qur'an, created man, taught him eloquence' culminates in the seventh rank of bayan. Fourth, he highlights the pairing of 'Read' and 'your Lord is the Most Generous' and the wisdom in not repeating 'Read in the name of your Lord', but rather emphasizing the generosity of the Lord. Fifth, he marvels at how few letters and words carry such profound meaning without loss of clarity, with noble composition and sound arrangement. He concludes that the entire Qur'an is miraculous for those ignorant of eloquence, but its i'jaz is recognized at different levels: for some, the whole Qur'an; for others, ten suras; for others, one sura; and even one single verse can be sufficient. The degree of recognition depends on the person's knowledge (dirayah) and verification (tahqiq). Thus, the author affirms that i'jaz is not monolithic but graded according to the recipient's capacity.

Key Terms
إعجاز (i'jaz)
بلاغة (balaghah)
فصاحة (fasahah)
جزالة (jazalah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: I'jaz requires a long sura or many verses.
Correction: Shahristani shows that even the shortest verse (the first revelation) is laden with eloquence and wisdom, demonstrating that i'jaz is present in any part of the Quran, regardless of length.

Checkpoint
What does Shahristani say about those who restrict i'jaz to a long sura?
A) They are correct
B) They have fallen short
C) They are exaggerating
D) They are ignorant

Key Takeaway
The Quran's inimitability is not limited to length; even the shortest verses can be supremely eloquent and miraculous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever glossed over a short verse like 'Read in the name of your Lord who created' as too simple to contain deep meaning? After seeing Shahristani's analysis, what specific attitude will you change the next time you recite that verse? Write down a practical step to pause and ponder its layers tomorrow.
?
In your own speech or writing, do you assume that longer is more profound? How does the Qur'an's example challenge that assumption? What would it look like for you to strive for both conciseness and depth in your daily communications, especially when speaking about Allah?
?
When you reflect on the first revealed verse, do you truly feel awe at its comprehensiveness? If not, what does that reveal about your current relationship with the Qur'an? Commit to one concrete action this week to engage more deeply with the verses you might consider 'ordinary'.

Divine Transcendence

The Wisdom in the First Revelation: Generality and Specificity
Shahristani details the rhetorical and theological layers in the first revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Shahristani analyzes the first revelation to reveal layers of meaning regarding God's lordship, creation, and the stages of revelation.

Core Idea
The Quran's conciseness conceals profound theological depth.

Shahristani continues his analysis of the first revelation, pointing out specific rhetorical features: the use of the name 'Rabb' (Lord) in the context of nurturing, the pattern of generalization and specification, and a parallel with Surah al-Rahman that mirrors the stages of human development and revelation. This lesson explores these insights.

Guided Path
1
Observe the specification of 'Rabb'
Read: 'ثم انظر كيف خصص اسم الربوبية في حال تربيته'. Notice that 'Rabb' is used specifically in the context of tarbiyah (nurturing).
The name 'Rabb' implies a nurturing relationship.
2
Track the pattern of generality and specificity
Notice how creation is first generalized ('خلق') then specified to man ('خلق الإنسان'). Similarly, learning is generalized then specified.
This is a recurring rhetorical pattern in the Quran.
3
Understand the parallel with Surah al-Rahman
Read the reference to Surah al-Rahman 1-4. Shahristani links the seven stages of revelation (balagh) to the seven ranks of human development from a clot.
The seventh rank is the reception of bayan (eloquence).
4
Reflect on the wisdom
Consider why this parallel exists. It shows the Quran's coherence and its correspondence to human nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Iqra bismi Rabbik', do you truly feel that your Lord is personally nurturing you through each verse? If not, what is blocking you from receiving that intimate tarbiyah? Will you pause before your next recitation to ask Him to open your heart to that nurturing?
?
The revelation came in stages, just as a clot develops into a complete human. Are you trying to rush your spiritual growth, or do you embrace the gradual process that God has designed? Which stage are you currently in, and what is the next step you need to take today?
?
Shahristani draws a parallel between your physical creation from a clot and your reception of divine knowledge. How does this change the way you view your own body and your learning of the Qur'an? Commit to one concrete change in how you approach the Qur'an this week—will you read more slowly, reflect more deeply, or seek to connect each verse to your own growth?

The Graded Nature of I'jaz According to Knowledge
Shahristani concludes that i'jaz is recognized at different levels based on knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Shahristani's conclusion that the Quran's inimitability is recognized at different levels, depending on the person's knowledge and verification.

Core Idea
The miracle of the Quran is perceived in degrees based on one's depth of understanding.

After his detailed analysis of the first revelation, Shahristani draws a broader conclusion: the Quran as a whole is miraculous, but people recognize its i'jaz to varying extents. Some see it in the entire Quran, others in ten suras, one sura, or even a single verse. The measure of recognition depends on one's knowledge (dirayah) and verification (tahqiq). This lesson explores this graded concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding passage
Read from 'فتبين من هذه القرائن' to the end. Notice the summary statement that the whole Quran is miraculous.
2
Identify the four levels of recognition
Spot the four categories: the whole Quran, ten suras, one sura, one verse. They correspond to different levels of understanding.
Shahristani says: 'فتبين من هذه القرائن أن القرآن بجملته معجز، وذلك في حق من لا يعرف دقائق البلاغة وحقائق المعاني، وبعشر سور منه معجز في حق قوم، وبسورة واحدة معجز في حق قوم، وبآية واحدة معجز في حق قوم'.
3
Understand the condition for recognition
Read the last sentence: 'وتتقدر مقادير الإعجاز على قدر الدراية والتحقيق'. The degree of i'jaz is measured by one's knowledge (dirayah) and verification (tahqiq).
This means i'jaz is objective but its perception is subjective.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this refutes the idea that i'jaz is only for experts or only for the masses. It is for everyone, but each according to his capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And how He linked {Read, and your Lord is the Most Generous, who taught by the pen, taught man what he did not know} [al-'Alaq 96:3-5]. So the wisdom in 'Read' first and second, and {your Lord is the Most Generous} is more eloquent in wording and meaning than {Read in the name of your Lord}, then He generalized learning then specified man, just as He generalized creation then specified man. And how eloquence (fasahah) gathered in words with indication of aspects of meanings, and how it was accompanied by this grandeur (jazalah) despite the fewness of its letters and lightness of its words without deficiency in indication, and how it was organized in the most noble composition in terms of wording, and the soundest arrangement in terms of meaning, and how eloquence (balaghah) appeared in it with freshness of words and solidity of meanings until it seemed to have gathered the knowledge of the earlier and later generations from the establishment of generality and specificity, and the precedence of command over creation, and the attribution of guidance and creation to Him {Blessed is Allah, Lord of the worlds} [Ghafir 40:64]. So it becomes clear from these indicators that the entire Qur'an is miraculous (mu'jiz), and that is for those who do not know the subtleties of eloquence and the realities of meanings; and by ten suras of it it is miraculous for some people; and by one sura it is miraculous for some people; and by one verse it is miraculous for some people. And the measures of inimitability (i'jaz) are estimated according to the measure of knowledge (dirayah) and verification (tahqiq).
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage is an argument demonstrating the inimitability (i'jaz) of the Qur'an, responding to those who restrict it to long suras. The author (Shahristani) begins by criticizing those who limit the Qur'an's miraculous nature to long suras or specific verses, asserting that they have fallen short. He then directs attention to the very first revealed verse (Surah al-'Alaq, 96:1-2) as a counterexample. He analyzes its eloquence in several aspects: first, if one wanted to express God's creativity both general and specific (the whole world and man as a microcosm), no wording could be more eloquent and concise. Second, he observes the specification of the name 'Lord' (al-Rabb) in the context of tarbiyah (nurturing), the generalization of creation followed by specification of man, and the beginning of man's creation from a clot—likening the stages of revelation (the seven stations of receiving the Qur'an) to the stages of human development. Third, he notes the parallelism with Surah al-Rahman, where the sequence 'taught the Qur'an, created man, taught him eloquence' culminates in the seventh rank of bayan. Fourth, he highlights the pairing of 'Read' and 'your Lord is the Most Generous' and the wisdom in not repeating 'Read in the name of your Lord', but rather emphasizing the generosity of the Lord. Fifth, he marvels at how few letters and words carry such profound meaning without loss of clarity, with noble composition and sound arrangement. He concludes that the entire Qur'an is miraculous for those ignorant of eloquence, but its i'jaz is recognized at different levels: for some, the whole Qur'an; for others, ten suras; for others, one sura; and even one single verse can be sufficient. The degree of recognition depends on the person's knowledge (dirayah) and verification (tahqiq). Thus, the author affirms that i'jaz is not monolithic but graded according to the recipient's capacity.

Key Terms
إعجاز (i'jaz)
دراية (dirayah)
تحقيق (tahqiq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: I'jaz is either present or absent uniformly for all people.
Correction: Shahristani states that i'jaz is recognized at different levels (whole Quran, ten suras, one sura, one verse) depending on the person's knowledge and verification.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, on what does the degree of recognition of i'jaz depend?
A) The length of the sura
B) The number of verses read
C) The person's knowledge and verification
D) The chronological order of revelation

Key Takeaway
The Quran's inimitability is one reality, but its recognition varies according to each person's depth of knowledge and insight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a single verse of the Quran, do you experience it as a miracle? If not, what does that reveal about your level of 'dirayah and tahqiq' as described by Shahristani? Commit to studying one verse this week until its inimitability becomes apparent to you.
?
Shahristani says the entire Quran is miraculous for those who know nothing of eloquence. Is that a comfort or a challenge to you? Are you content with the basic level, or are you striving for deeper knowledge to see the miracle in fewer words?
?
What specific knowledge (dirayah) do you need to acquire to move from seeing the Quran as miraculous in its entirety to seeing its inimitability in a single verse? Write down one step you will take today towards that goal.

Divine Transcendence
beginner
4 min
The Analogy of Manifestation: Meaning and Expression
Analogy of mental meaning to speech, writing, mirror, and shadow.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how mental meanings are manifested in physical expressions without inherence, using the examples of speech, writing, mirror, and shadow.

Core Idea
Meanings are shown through words and images without residing in them.

The author begins with a simple yet profound analogy to explain how the spiritual relates to the physical. Just as the meaning in your mind appears in spoken words without physically moving into them, so too do higher realities manifest in lower forms. This analogy sets the stage for the discussion of divine transcendence and the nature of prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the two examples
The passage presents two comparisons: first, mental meaning expressed in speech and writing; second, the image in a mirror and shadow on water. See how each illustrates manifestation without inherence.
3
Understand the key phrase 'without inherence'
The author explicitly denies inherence (hulul) and descent (nuzul). Ask yourself why this is crucial for the overall argument about divine transcendence.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثالا : تمثل المعنى الذي في الذهن بالعبارات التي في اللسان، وبالكتابة التي في اللوح، غير أن اللفظ دليل على المعنى وكل دليل مظهر المعنى من وجه، وكل مظهر دال على المعنى من وجه من غير حلول الأعراض في الجواهر، ولا نزول الأجسام على الأجسام، وإن تخطيت منه إلى ظهور صورة الشخص في المرآة الصقيلة، ووقوع الظل على الماء الصافي، قارنت المعنى مثالاً وإدراكًا. ألستم تقولون معاشر الصابئة المحيلين نزول الروحاني على الجسماني: إن كل روحاني له هيكل يظهر به ويتصرف فيه ويستوي عليه، وكل هيكل في العالم العلوي له شخص في العالم السفلي يظهر به ويتصرف فيه ويستوي عليه، غير أنكم اتخذتم بصنعتكم أصناما آلهة وهي مظاهر تلك الهياكل التي هي مظاهر تلك الروحانيات؟ ونحن معاشر الحنفاء أثبتنا أشخاصاً ربانية مفطورة بوضع الخلقة على اصطفاء من الجواهر واجتباء من العنصر مناسبة لتلك الجواهر الروحانية مناسبة النور للنور والظل للظل.
An example: The meaning that is in the mind is represented by the expressions that are on the tongue, and by the writing that is on the tablet, except that the utterance is an indication (dalil) of the meaning, and every indication is a manifestation (muzhir) of the meaning in some respect, and every manifestation is an indicator (dal) of the meaning in some respect, without the inherence (hulul) of accidents in substances, nor the descent (nuzul) of bodies upon bodies. And if you go beyond that to the appearance of the image of a person in a polished mirror, and the falling of a shadow on clear water, you will have linked the meaning as an example and an apprehension. Do you not say, O community of Sabians who forbid the descent of the spiritual upon the corporeal: That every spiritual being (ruhani) has a temple (haykal) through which it appears, acts, and sits upon, and every temple in the upper world has a person (shakhs) in the lower world through which it appears, acts, and sits upon? Yet you have taken, by your own craft, idols as gods, and they are manifestations of those temples which are manifestations of those spiritual beings? And we, the community of Hanifas, have affirmed spiritual persons (ashkhas rabbaniyya) created by the constitution of creation, chosen from substances and selected from the element, suitable for those spiritual substances in the manner of the suitability of light for light and shadow for shadow.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage is an analogical argument and a refutation of the Sabians. The author begins by explaining the relationship between mental meanings and their physical representations: the meaning is represented by spoken words and written script, but the word is an indication and manifestation of the meaning without implying that the meaning physically inhabits the word. Similarly, the image in a mirror and the shadow on water are manifestations without physical transfer. Then he addresses the Sabians, who reject the descent of the spiritual onto the corporeal, yet they themselves hold that every spiritual being has a physical temple through which it appears and acts. They even make idols as manifestations of spiritual beings. The author contrasts this with the Hanifa position: they affirm that there are 'spiritual persons' (possibly prophets) created by God, chosen from substances, suitable for the spiritual substances like light to light and shadow to shadow. This sets up the discussion of the Prophet's nature.

Key Terms
مثال (analogy)
دليل (indication)
مظهر (manifestation)
حلول (inherence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Spiritual meanings or realities physically reside in physical expressions like words or images.
Correction: According to the author, they are manifested without inherence; the physical expression indicates and manifests the meaning without containing it.

Checkpoint
In the analogy, what does the author say about the relationship between a word and its meaning?
A) The meaning inheres in the word like an accident in a substance.
B) The word is a manifestation and indication of the meaning without inherence.
C) The word is identical to the meaning.
D) The word is separate and unrelated to the meaning.

Key Takeaway
Manifestation does not require physical indwelling; meanings appear through expressions without residing in them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran or perform acts of worship, do you ever feel that the outward action or word itself is what matters most, rather than the inner meaning it is meant to indicate? How can this analogy refocus your intention?
?
If manifestation does not require physical indwelling, how should this change your understanding of God's presence? Are there times you have limited God to a place or symbol instead of recognizing His manifestation without containment?
?
In your daily interactions, do you confuse the symbol with the reality—for example, mistaking a person's words for their actual inner state? How can this lesson teach you to look beyond expressions to the meanings they indicate?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate

Refutation of the Sabians and the Hanifa Position
Refutation of Sabians and presentation of Hanifa view on spiritual persons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sabian view on spiritual-corporeal relationship, the inconsistency in their practice of idol worship, and the Hanifa affirmation of special spiritual persons.

Core Idea
The Sabians reject spiritual descent but use idols as manifestations, contradicting themselves.

The author now turns to a specific sect, the Sabians, who deny that spiritual beings can descend into corporeal forms. Yet, they themselves hold that every spiritual being has a physical temple and even make idols as manifestations. The author points out this inconsistency and then presents the Hanifa position, which affirms special created persons suitable for spiritual substances, using the analogy of light to light and shadow to shadow.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Sabian view
Read the first part about the Sabian belief that every spiritual being has a temple and a lower person through which it appears, acts, and sits. Note their denial of descent.
2
Identify the contradiction
The author challenges them: despite denying descent, you make idols as manifestations of those temples/spiritual beings. This is the core of the refutation.
Consider why this is a logical inconsistency.
3
Contrast with the Hanifa view
The Hanifas affirm created spiritual persons (ashkhas rabbaniyya) chosen from substances, suitable for spiritual substances like light to light.
This is presented as the correct alternative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثالا : تمثل المعنى الذي في الذهن بالعبارات التي في اللسان، وبالكتابة التي في اللوح، غير أن اللفظ دليل على المعنى وكل دليل مظهر المعنى من وجه، وكل مظهر دال على المعنى من وجه من غير حلول الأعراض في الجواهر، ولا نزول الأجسام على الأجسام، وإن تخطيت منه إلى ظهور صورة الشخص في المرآة الصقيلة، ووقوع الظل على الماء الصافي، قارنت المعنى مثالاً وإدراكًا. ألستم تقولون معاشر الصابئة المحيلين نزول الروحاني على الجسماني: إن كل روحاني له هيكل يظهر به ويتصرف فيه ويستوي عليه، وكل هيكل في العالم العلوي له شخص في العالم السفلي يظهر به ويتصرف فيه ويستوي عليه، غير أنكم اتخذتم بصنعتكم أصناما آلهة وهي مظاهر تلك الهياكل التي هي مظاهر تلك الروحانيات؟ ونحن معاشر الحنفاء أثبتنا أشخاصاً ربانية مفطورة بوضع الخلقة على اصطفاء من الجواهر واجتباء من العنصر مناسبة لتلك الجواهر الروحانية مناسبة النور للنور والظل للظل.
An example: The meaning that is in the mind is represented by the expressions that are on the tongue, and by the writing that is on the tablet, except that the utterance is an indication (dalil) of the meaning, and every indication is a manifestation (muzhir) of the meaning in some respect, and every manifestation is an indicator (dal) of the meaning in some respect, without the inherence (hulul) of accidents in substances, nor the descent (nuzul) of bodies upon bodies. Do you not say, O community of Sabians who forbid the descent of the spiritual upon the corporeal: That every spiritual being (ruhani) has a temple (haykal) through which it appears, acts, and sits upon, and every temple in the upper world has a person (shakhs) in the lower world through which it appears, acts, and sits upon? Yet you have taken, by your own craft, idols as gods, and they are manifestations of those temples which are manifestations of those spiritual beings? And we, the community of Hanifas, have affirmed spiritual persons (ashkhas rabbaniyya) created by the constitution of creation, chosen from substances and selected from the element, suitable for those spiritual substances in the manner of the suitability of light for light and shadow for shadow.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage is an analogical argument and a refutation of the Sabians. The author begins by explaining the relationship between mental meanings and their physical representations: the meaning is represented by spoken words and written script, but the word is an indication and manifestation of the meaning without implying that the meaning physically inhabits the word. Key Terms
صابئة (Sabians)
حنفاء (Hanifas)
هيكل (temple)
شخص (person)
روحاني (spiritual)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What contradiction does the author point out in the Sabian position?
A) They deny spiritual descent but affirm the existence of God.
B) They deny spiritual descent yet make idols as manifestations of spiritual beings.
C) They worship idols but claim to be monotheists.
D) They believe in temples but not in persons.

Key Takeaway
The Hanifa position affirms a special created suitability between spiritual persons and spiritual substances, avoiding the inconsistency of the Sabians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself holding a belief that your actions contradict, like the Sabians denying spiritual descent yet worshipping idols? In what specific area of your life might you be claiming one thing but doing another? What will you change today to bring your actions into alignment with your faith?
?
The Hanifas affirm that spiritual persons are created by God and specially suited for receiving spiritual substances. Do you see yourself as having a unique suitability for divine guidance? What practical steps will you take to cultivate that suitability in your daily life?
?
The Sabians made idols with their own hands and called them gods. Are there any 'idols' in your heart—people, possessions, or ambitions—that have taken God's rightful place? How will you actively remove them and redirect your devotion to Him alone?
